# Kritik---Security---Starter Packet

### Notes

While the security K is a tried-and-true classic generic position, this
version is a little more topic-specific. The security K you may have
heard or read before is usually not very concerned with the mechanism of
the aff or the internal links; it's usually more about the aff's
representations or discourse. This version is still about those things,
but I tried to make the evidence as specific to the Arctic as possible,
because there's actually quite a bit of literature about securitization
theory in the Arctic context specifically.

On that note, it's worth mentioning that you will be most successful
with this K if you familiarize yourself with the background literature.
Often, the security K gets distilled down into "you talked about
threats, don't do that," which is both boring and unstrategic. The
security K is not just the argument that "threat discourse=bad," but a
more nuanced criticism of how we understand states' motivations and
policy choices. Some questions that I would encourage you to feel
comfortable answering before extending this in a 2NR include:

- What is the Copenhagen School?

- What is constructivism?

- What is realism (and what are classical realism, offensive/defensive
  realism, neorealism, etc), and why is it relevant to this K?

- Does the aff assume the tenets of realism, liberalism, or some other
  theory?

- What's the difference between discourse and epistemology?

A good starting point is this dissertation by Wilfrid Greaves (which
some cards in this file are from); it's long, but not all of it is
relevant for this K, and it has good background primers on many of the
above questions:
<https://utoronto.scholaris.ca/items/e0790f25-319a-401b-a8c2-3149989fbc22>

I've intentionally included fewer blocks and much less highlighting in
this file than I have in kritiks that I've written for starter packets
in previous seasons. This is because I wrote this K as a way of
practicing framework debates and link debates, as those are where this K
is won and lost. I think it's tremendously important to practice
adapting your blocks and highlighting to different affs and aff
strategies. If you put in the work to understand the literature and
spend some time thinking through what you're saying and why, rather than
just reading pre-written blocks, you will be much more successful over
the season and over the course of your career. When prepping this file,
my three biggest pieces of advice are:

1.  **Be intentional with how you write your framework frontlines.**
    What do debates look like under your interpretation? How does the
    aff win, and how does the judge decide that? Interpretations that
    answer these questions explicitly are usually stronger than things
    like "the 1AC is an object of study" or "the role of the judge is to
    reject securitization." Also remember that **this is not a K of
    fiat**, and it's neither necessary nor helpful to use this as a
    vehicle for a hidden fiat K in the block (which is why I
    intentionally avoided including cards for that in this file).

2.  **Your best links are case defense.** If you are right about your
    defense, then it proves that the 1AC has either inflated a threat
    and/or offered a hasty solution that may make the threat they've
    outlined worse rather than better. Either way, it proves the
    importance of figuring out how that happened before we decide on
    solutions.

3.  **Your best impacts are in the 1AC.** Following from #2, you should
    focus on "turns case" arguments, as they make it a lot harder for
    the aff to go for "link=non-unique" arguments (which are otherwise
    quite threatening).

For the aff, keep in mind that many of the cards here will probably not
be necessary. Your best defenses of your representations will likely be
in the 1AC! I'd also encourage you to press on link uniqueness. Think
about how your advantages/impact scenarios are examples of the links
occurring in the status quo, or otherwise represent alt causes to the
link. Depending on the aff, you can also press substantively on the
alt---this K is written to explicitly defend cooperative, demilitarized
engagement in the Arctic, so if your aff says that those things are
impossible, then the 1AC is 8 minutes of disads to the alt.

## Topshelf

### K---1NC

#### The 1AC [over-securitizes]{.underline} the Arctic, making militarized conflict [inevitable]{.underline} and preventing cooperation over [non-military]{.underline} existential threats. Vote neg to [reject]{.underline} the 1AC's security framing---reps come [first]{.underline}.

**Gricius, 21**---PhD student at Colorado State University and Graduate
Fellow at the North American & Arctic Defense and Security Network
(Gabriella, "The Arctic's Securitization,"
<https://inkstickmedia.com/the-arctics-securitization/>, dml)

Whether you read US Army and Coastal Guard Strategies from other Arctic
states or the front page of the New York Times, [the Arctic is becoming
nearly synonymous with **fears**]{.underline} about Russia and China.
Military [strategists and journalists]{.underline} alike [write about
**Russian militarization** as well as unwelcome **Chinese
influence**]{.underline} in the High North. [While there are certainly
**geopolitical realities** to take into consideration when thinking
about Arctic policy]{.underline}, [in [**focusing too heavily**
on]{.mark} questions of]{.underline} "**[hard]{.underline}**"
[[security]{.underline}]{.mark}, [policymakers are **obscuring** other
transnational issues]{.underline}, [such as **climate change** and
**cyber security**]{.underline}, [where cooperation is not only desired
but also necessary]{.underline}.

In other words, [discussing interstate competition **too much** [leads
the Arctic]{.mark} on a **trajectory** [toward becoming]{.mark} a
**[securitized]{.mark} region**]{.underline}. [Yet]{.underline}, **[it
doesn't have to be this way]{.underline}**. [This trend can]{.underline}
--- [and should]{.underline} --- [be **reversed** to **mitigate**
possible consequences]{.underline}, [such as]{.underline}: [Focusing on
how cooperation can be used to **solve key issues** in the Arctic rather
than thinking solely about **military buildup**]{.underline}; [changing
the **framing** of the Arctic in official policies]{.underline},
[speeches]{.underline}, [and critical international
organizations]{.underline}, such as the Arctic Council, [and finding
**low-stakes issues** to encourage relationship-building]{.underline}
among actors in the Arctic.

So, can the Arctic be spared from securitization?

EARLY SYMPTOMS OF SECURITIZATION

[[Securitization]{.underline}]{.mark} is the process of something
becoming a security threat or a security issue. It [[happens when
something]{.underline}]{.mark} --- such as immigration --- [[is labeled
as]{.mark} **dangerous**]{.underline}, **[threatening]{.underline}**,
[and as [an **existential crisis**]{.mark} by someone]{.underline},
[[and]{.mark} then [that framing is **accepted** by an
audience]{.mark}]{.underline}. Importantly, securitization implies that
security issues are not just out there. Instead, [by creating security
**threats** in the way we **speak**]{.underline}, [we create security
**issues**]{.underline}. While the Arctic might seem like a peripheral
region globally, there is evidence that it is beginning to rise in
importance. [[Concerns about **China**]{.underline},
**[Russia]{.underline}**, [and **resource competition** are causing
**real changes** to occur in]{.underline}]{.mark} [how states treat [the
Arctic]{.mark}]{.underline}. The United States, for example, has
announced the establishment of the Arctic Regional Center, which will be
housed within the Department of Defense and focus on the "unique
challenges and security concerns related to the Arctic region."

As climate change has resulted in new shipping lanes and easier access
to resources in the Arctic, the interests of Arctic states --- United
States, Russia, Canada, Norway, Iceland, Finland, Denmark, and Sweden
--- and non-Arctic states --- China, United Kingdom, and EU to name a
few --- have grown. The Arctic presents virtually untapped amounts of
oil, gas, and minerals that attract both Arctic and non-Arctic states. A
United States Geological Survey 2008 report also described the Arctic
continental shelves as potentially constituting "the geographically
largest unexplored prospective area for petroleum remaining on Earth."
While it is true that some states, such as China, are finding investment
opportunities with Arctic states to build infrastructure as part of the
Polar Silk Road (part of the Belt and Road Initiative), [[it is **by no
means a necessity** to treat **Chinese** influence as **inherently
negative**]{.underline}]{.mark}. By the same logic,
[**[Russian]{.mark}** investment and military [actions]{.mark} in the
Arctic [might **seem**]{.mark} overtly [negative]{.mark}]{.underline},
[[but it is important to **contextualize**]{.mark} Russian interest in
the Arctic as not recent]{.underline}, [but historic]{.underline}.
[Instead]{.underline}, [the rhetoric around these issues has treated
them as **critical security risks**]{.underline}, [painting the Arctic
as the next theater of a **new Cold War**]{.underline}. But is the
Arctic really the next theater?

[Rather than focusing on these interstate threats]{.underline}, [[there
are **more serious concerns**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[such as **climate
change**]{.mark} and]{.underline} insecurities that stem from it ---
such as **[[food]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
**[[water]{.underline}]{.mark}**,
**[[environmental]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [and [**health**
security]{.mark} concerns]{.underline} --- [that affect individuals and
impact their quality of life]{.underline}. For example, Alaskan
[communities are suffering from **food insecurity**]{.underline} because
sea ice is melting, causing traditional sea mammals that they would
otherwise hunt, to move elsewhere [[and]{.underline}]{.mark} decreasing
their protection from the large ocean waves that have in some cases
caused [devastating **[infrastructure damage]{.mark}**]{.underline}. On
Little Diomede Island, for example, a late storm caused serious damage
to a water treatment plant, leaving the town without clean water and
without power. [By **[over-emphasizing]{.mark}**]{.underline}
"**[[hard]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [[security]{.mark} issues]{.underline}
from Russia and China, therefore, [the rhetoric surrounding Arctic
politics **[ignores]{.mark}** other important [questions that impact
people on the ground]{.mark}]{.underline}, [particularly **Indigenous
people** whose livelihoods and societal resilience are threatened by
climate change]{.underline}.

[The most significant concern]{.underline} when it comes to treating the
Arctic as a theater of great power competition [is the
**danger**]{.underline} it presents [in putting the Arctic on a
**trajectory** of becoming a **securitized region**]{.underline}.
[[While]{.underline}]{.mark} it can be argued that as it stands, [[the
Arctic is not]{.mark} a **contested** region or a region **[fraught with
wars]{.mark}**]{.underline}, such as the Middle East and parts of
Africa, [[it **could be** if the trajectory]{.mark} of treating the
Arctic as this region of competition and security
**[continues]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [[Regions]{.mark} of the world [do
not become theaters of competition]{.mark} and conflict
**[overnight]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[but]{.underline}]{.mark} instead
[become that way [after]{.mark} years of **[construction]{.mark}** that
involves a range of activities from **rhetoric** and to **military
buildups**]{.underline}. [[**Crimea** is]{.mark} a telling
[example]{.mark} of this]{.underline}, [[where]{.mark} both Ukraine and
Russia slowly began ratcheting up **[bellicose discourse]{.mark}** over
time which [was followed by **militarization**]{.mark}]{.underline}.
Now, Crimea is a point of severe tension between the two states.

[The Arctic]{.underline}, however, [is not]{.underline} --- [and should
not]{.underline} --- [be destined for that end state]{.underline}.
[We]{.underline}, the journalists who report on our news, and the
policymakers who create reports, [have a **choice** in how we **frame**
issues]{.underline}. [Rather than always treating the Arctic as a place
where competition is **inherent**]{.underline}, [we can **instead** see
it as a place of possible **cooperation**]{.underline}. After all, [the
largest security threat in the Arctic is **climate
change**]{.underline}. [It impacts not only the people living in the
Arctic itself]{.underline}, [but also]{.underline} states surrounding
the Arctic, and the rest of [the **world**]{.underline}, [as sea and
ocean levels increase]{.underline}, [oceans become
desalinated]{.underline}, [and temperatures increase
worldwide]{.underline}. [There is a **choice** to focus on how states
can and have been **cooperating** on this issue]{.underline}, [thereby
**incentivizing further collaboration** down the line instead of
thinking solely on old-school ways of thinking about
security]{.underline}.

HOW TO AVOID THE SECURITIZATION TRAJECTORY

[While we **can** think and write about geopolitical hard security
issues in the Arctic]{.underline}, [we can do so in a way that **also**
gives airtime to **cooperation** and **collaboration** rather than
thinking solely about the latest **Russian military exercise** or most
recent scare about **Chinese investment** in Greenland]{.underline}.
[This is **not** to say that reporting on Russia and China is not
important or necessary]{.underline}, [but rather]{.underline},
[reporting in a **balanced** way that takes the responsibility of
reporting **all** sides of an issue seriously]{.underline}.

On a policy level, [policymakers]{.underline} and diplomats
**[can]{.underline}** --- [and **should**]{.underline} --- [**change the
framing** of the Arctic in official policies]{.underline},
[speeches]{.underline}, [and critical international
organizations]{.underline}, such as the Arctic Council. [Security
shouldn't be only about the]{.underline} construction of new ships,
submarines, and **[military]{.underline}** exercises. [Planners who are
thinking about the role of militaries and institutions should consider
how security also refers to]{.underline} the
**[individual]{.underline}** security of Arctic residents, focusing on
their ability to access food [and]{.underline} water, potential
insecurities arising from coastal erosion --- 70% of which in the Arctic
is built on currently thawing permafrost --- as well as [**societal**
insecurities]{.underline} that come about from a lack of community
resilience.

Lastly, [[there is a tendency]{.underline}]{.mark} in international
politics [[to focus on **conflict**]{.mark}**ual** issues [when]{.mark}
instead [we should]{.mark} be [find]{.mark}ing other]{.underline},
[[more **cooperative** issues]{.mark} to encourage relationship-building
among actors in the Arctic]{.underline}. [Instead of immediately
pivoting to trying to negotiate **arms control treaties** amongst Arctic
states]{.underline}, [countries could instead find issues]{.underline},
[[such as **transparency**]{.underline}]{.mark},
**[[s]{.underline}]{.mark}**earch **[[a]{.underline}]{.mark}**nd
**[[r]{.underline}]{.mark}**escue [cooperation]{.underline}, [[and
**pollution** clean-up]{.mark} that does not require as much political
capital]{.underline}. [[These]{.mark} issues [might be framed as
**low-stakes** but they should not be]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly
if all stakeholders want [[to **ensure** that the Arctic is **not
securitized**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

**[RHETORIC MATTERS]{.underline}** AFTER ALL

[[It **matters** how we talk about the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark}.
[[**Whaling** is a great example]{.mark} of [where changing rhetoric has
**impacted policy**]{.mark}]{.underline}. While in the first half of the
20th century, whaling was considered a fairly normal aspect of the
economy, [anti-whaling discourse changed the depiction of
whales]{.underline} to intelligent mammals. [This **transformed** the
perception of whaling to morally repugnant]{.underline}. It goes to show
that [[the way we talk **matters**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [and it can
matter **[more than material interests]{.mark}** that might seem more
logical]{.underline}.

[We can continue to use **[bellicose rhetoric]{.mark}** to describe
relations between states and [encourage **militarization**]{.mark} on
every side]{.underline}, [but [that will end]{.mark} **exactly** [the
way worse-case scenarios describe it]{.mark}]{.underline}.
[Instead]{.underline}, [[we]{.mark} can]{.underline} --- [and
[should]{.mark}]{.underline} --- [[**change** the way we **talk** and
**think** about the Arctic]{.mark} [to focus on
**cooperation**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [change the way we **frame** the
region]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} find low-stakes issues on which to
**cooperate** and]{.underline} hopefully [[**reverse** or]{.mark} at
least [**slow** the trajectory]{.mark} of securitizing the
Arctic]{.underline}.

### Impact---2NC

#### Preventing [competition]{.underline} from dominating Arctic narratives is key to [cooperation]{.underline} over [existential threats]{.underline}. Securitization's not [inevitable]{.underline} or [irreversible]{.underline}. 

**Heggelund, 23**---Master's candidate in International Affairs, School
of Social Sciences, University of Iceland (Ragna Mathiesen, "Thawing
Arctic Realities: Analyzing Arctic Security through the Lens of Arctic
Exceptionalism and Great Power Politics,"
<https://skemman.is/handle/1946/45722>, dml)

Furthermore, [shared]{.underline} knowledge and [interpretations play a
**crucial role** in shaping the perceptions]{.underline},
[behaviors]{.underline}, [and interactions of actors in the global
system]{.underline} (Flockhart, 2016, p. 85). [The AC's success in
diplomatic negotiations and its ability to foster cooperation among
Arctic states is an example of how]{.underline} shared knowledge and
[interpretations **shape perceptions** of the Arctic as a region
requiring cooperative solutions]{.underline} (Jóhannesson, 2022). [This
success in the Arctic has implications for **global diplomacy** and the
importance of cooperative forums in addressing **shared
challenges**]{.underline}. Furthermore, the [scientific cooperation
within the AC aimed at better environmental protection and sustainable
development exemplifies how shared knowledge and interpretations
influence **global scientific collaboration**]{.underline} (Jóhannesson,
2022). [Research findings and knowledge sharing in the Arctic contribute
to **global efforts** to combat **climate change** and protect the
**environment**]{.underline}. Furthermore, [the **exclusion** of **hard
security** matters]{.underline} from the AC agenda [reflects **shared
interpretations** among Arctic states that the region should remain
**primarily focused** on non-military cooperation and environmental
concerns]{.underline} (Exner-Pirot et al., 2019). It aligns with Wendt's
(1992) perspective regrading principles of norms and shared
understandings. [This constructivist perspective helps us recognize how
the Arctic cooperative narrative **emerges**]{.underline},
**[spreads]{.underline}**, [and becomes **internalized** as a framework
for understanding the region's security dynamics]{.underline}.

In relation to great power competition [the increasing interest of
**great powers**]{.underline} like the US, Russia, and China [in the
Arctic]{.underline} due to economic opportunities and strategic
considerations (Gross, 2020; Lopez, 2021; Humpert, 2022) [shows how
shared knowledge and interpretations about the Arctic's changing
landscape can **influence** their global strategies]{.underline}. [This
competition in the Arctic could have implications for **global power
dynamics**]{.underline} and international relations. Wendt's (1992)
argument that structures manifest through formal rules and norms is
particularly relevant. [States' behaviors and interactions are
influenced by norms and rules associated with their **identities** and
**social groups**]{.underline}. [In the context of great power
competition]{.underline}, [norms related to cooperation and rivalry are
**constructed** and **internalized** over time]{.underline}.
Furthermore, Wendt's perspective reminds us that [the
Arctic's]{.underline} evolving structure, influenced by [norms
associated with cooperation and rivalry]{.underline}, [plays a **pivotal
role** in shaping how states respond to the challenges posed by the
melting Arctic and the dynamics of great power competition]{.underline}.

5.1.3 Identity and Logic of Actions

In constructivism, identities establish order and predictability in
domestic society and international politics (Hopf, 1998). [The presence
of **inter-subjective identities**]{.underline}, characterized by
stability and consistency, [forms the basis for **enduring
expectations** among states]{.underline}, [guiding their
behavior]{.underline}. [Arctic cooperation]{.underline}, as viewed
through a constructivist lens, [is influenced by]{.underline} the
identities of the participating states. The concept of inter-subjective
identities, characterized by stability and consistency, creates [a sense
of **order** and **predictability** in the region]{.underline} (Hopf,
1998). [Arctic states share an identity as Arctic actors]{.underline},
[emphasizing their **common interests** in environmental
preservation]{.underline}, [sustainable development]{.underline}, [and
cooperation in relation to the melting Arctic]{.underline} (Arctic
Council, n.d.-a). [This]{.underline} shared identity [forms the basis
for **enduring expectations** among states]{.underline}, [guiding their
behavior within the Arctic context]{.underline}.

[Even though the **US** and **Russia** have been able to work as
cooperative Arctic actors]{.underline} within the AC, one could argue
that, [as **great power competition** reenters the Arctic
region]{.underline}, [these Arctic cooperative actors may evolve back
into **confrontational superpowers**]{.underline}. This is amplified by
the behavior of the states the US, Russia, and China within the region
and their pursuit of economic gain and strategic consideration (Gross,
2020; Lopez, 2021; Humpert, 2022).

5.1.4 Agents and structures

As illustrated by Flockhart (2016, p. 88), the concept of mutual
constitution underscores the dynamic nature of how perspectives in the
Arctic region are constructed, perpetuated, and potentially transformed.
In the context of the melting Arctic, the notion of mutual constitution
central to constructivism becomes apparent. This perspective emphasizes
that [structures **influence** actors and are simultaneously **shaped**
by their practices]{.underline} (Giddens, 1991). Archer (1995) expands
on this concept by introducing "structuration," highlighting that
[agents **reproduce** and **transform** structures through their
actions]{.underline}. Consider, for instance, the changing Arctic
identities. [Historically]{.underline}, [Arctic states viewed themselves
**primarily** as]{.underline} the AC members [focused on **cooperative
governance**]{.underline} (Lackenbauer & Dean, 2021, p. 327).
[However]{.underline}, [as the region's resources become **more
accessible** and the potential for **great power competition**
increases]{.underline}, [these identities are evolving]{.underline}.
[States]{.underline} like the US [are adapting their practices and
identities to **assert themselves more forcefully** in the
region]{.underline}, [challenging the existing cooperative
structures]{.underline} (Flockhart, 2016, p. 88). The norms governing
Arctic security have also undergone transformation. The Arctic was
traditionally seen as a zone of cooperation, with a focus on
environmental protection and peaceful coexistence. However, the changing
geopolitical landscape and resource interests are reshaping these norms.
[The emergence of new security concerns related to resource competition
and military presence reflects how practices and interactions can
**influence security perspectives**]{.underline} (Archer, 1995).

Furthermore, disruptive events in the Arctic, such as the opening of new
shipping routes and resource extraction projects, challenge shared
meanings and existing structures of governance. These events expose the
inadequacy of traditional cooperative structures in addressing emerging
security issues. Arctic states must adapt their practices and identities
to address these challenges effectively (Flockhart, 2016, p. 90). This
demonstrates how the constructivist perspective, emphasizing the mutual
constitution of structures and actors, can help us understand the
evolving dynamics of Arctic security and cooperation in the face of
environmental changes and shifting power dynamics.

5.2 Arctic Exceptionalism Perspective

Before diving further into the analysis, this section will start of by
addressing how Arctic exceptionalism will be utilized in the discussion.
The theory of Arctic exceptionalism offers an insightful framework for
comprehending the distinctive characteristics of the Arctic region
(Lackenbauer & Dean, 2021, p. 327). This understanding provides
researchers with the tools to effectively identify and predict security
threats unique to the Arctic. However, authors and scholars such as
Gjørv and Hodgson (2019) argue that Arctic exceptionalism's narrow view
of Arctic security prevents it from taking meaningful actions to protect
the environment and human security. Furthermore, Melchiorre (2022)
contends that the Arctic is not exceptional and is instead a reflection
of the international order, suggesting that the "exceptional" idea of
the Arctic was an illusion. Nonetheless, the concept of Arctic
exceptionalism has predominantly been interpreted through the lens of
Gorbachev's speech, where he characterizes the Arctic as a "zone of
peace" (Gorbachev, 1987). Instead, by revisiting the origin of the
theoretical framework, which emphasized the distinction from other
regions due to extraordinary characteristics and the perspective of the
Arctic as a "land of tomorrow" requiring exceptional protection, this
revised interpretation of Arctic exceptionalism could offer valuable
insights into the security dynamics of the region (Lackenbauer & Dean,
2021; Young & Osherenko, 1989; Young, 1992). Revisiting this
origin-based interpretation of Arctic exceptionalism not only offers
insights into the security dynamics of the region but also unlocks a
fresh perspective on how its exceptional characteristics shape both the
challenges and opportunities it presents.

Considering these insights, understanding the security dynamics in the
Arctic necessitates a shifting focus from the usual narrative of Arctic
exceptionalism in favor of a strategy that considers the region's unique
characteristics and vulnerabilities. Recognizing the intricate interplay
between environmental, human, and traditional security components, we
see the Arctic as more than just a "zone of peace." The updated or
revisited understanding of Arctic exceptionalism motivates researchers
to look at security issues from various approaches.

Viewed through the Arctic exceptionalist lens, the rapid sea ice loss
and tundra thawing in the Arctic, occurring at a rate three times higher
than the global average, exemplify the region's exceptional response to
global climate change (Arctic Council, n.d.-a; World Wildlife Fund
Arctic, 2023). This distinctiveness underscores the exceptional nature
of the Arctic environment, a critical area for climate study and policy.
The increased frequency of forest fires, transformation of the Arctic
landscape, rising sea levels, and loss of biodiversity in response to
these climate changes are further examples of the Arctic exceptional
nature (Gigova & Paddison, 2023; Struzik, 2020; Greenpeace USA, 2015).
Notably, Arctic exceptionalism highlights that the Arctic's response to
global climate shifts differs significantly from other regions by the
intricate connection of climate change, environmental security, human
security, and the rise of geopolitical competition. Moreover, the
concept underscores that [the consequences of the melting Arctic extend
**beyond** its borders]{.underline}, [affecting ecosystems
**worldwide**]{.underline}. [This interconnectedness]{.underline},
highlighted by Arctic exceptionalism, [emphasizes the need for
**international cooperation** and]{.underline} policy actions to address
Arctic climate change as part of broader **[global]{.underline}**
climate **[efforts]{.underline}** (GreenFacts, 2010). [The exceptional
vulnerabilities and global ramifications of Arctic climate change
underscore the imperative for **responsible governance** and
**sustainable practices**]{.underline}, recognizing the Arctic's pivotal
role in the broader climate system and its impacts on human security
globally.

The concept of Arctic exceptionalism underscores the importance of
collaboration concerning the interconnectedness of global ecosystems and
the shared responsibility to address environmental challenges. [The
urgency to adopt sustainable practices becomes evident when considering
the potential ramifications of the Arctic's vulnerabilities on a
**global scale**]{.underline}. [As the Arctic region continues to
experience rapid changes]{.underline}, [it serves as a call to
action]{.underline} for individuals, communities, and nations [to work
**collaboratively** in mitigating **climate change**]{.underline},
[preserving **biodiversity**]{.underline}, [and promoting **sustainable
development** practices]{.underline}. [By understanding and addressing
the unique vulnerabilities of the Arctic, we can better appreciate the
imperative for **immediate** and **concerted efforts** to secure a
resilient and harmonious future for the Arctic region and the **entire
planet**]{.underline}. This idea finds resonance in the statement by
Lackenbauer and Dean (2021, p. 328), who describe the Arctic as a realm
of "compelling opportunities and exhilarating risk." Indeed, these
dynamics extend beyond the Arctic region to influence the rest of the
world.

Viewed through the lens of Arctic exceptionalism, the challenges faced
by Arctic residents due to the changing Arctic climate take on a unique
and exceptional character (Bludd, 2022; Reist et al., 2006). Indigenous
peoples experience the profound impacts of these changes on their
traditional livelihoods. Arctic exceptionalism highlights that the
Arctic's response to global climate shifts differs significantly from
other regions, as it encompasses environmental changes and deeply
intertwined human and cultural dimensions. Kornhuber et al. (2023) state
that even though Arctic exceptionalism is "blocked" in geopolitics, the
challenges the region faces remains exceptional. Therefore, investing
more in remote sensing initiatives for independent scientific data
collection, free from geopolitical tensions, could be considered
(ibid.). Moreover, engaging Arctic Indigenous Peoples in co-producing
knowledge on ecosystems, climate change, biodiversity, and environmental
development is crucial. Their involvement can enhance the understanding
of the Arctic\'s unique challenges and contribute to more sustainable
solutions within the framework of Arctic exceptionalism.

Greaves (2019) on the other hand argue how growing geopolitical
competition are breaking up the unified Arctic and creating three
distinct security regions across the polar area. This change is similar
to how global warming is transforming the Arctic Ocean into something
more like the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans, erasing the Arctic\'s
uniqueness. This shift marks the end of the post-Cold War era when the
Arctic was seen as separate from global power struggles. As the Arctic
warms and becomes less distinct from lower-latitude regions, its
politics and security increasingly mirror those in the south (ibid.).
Furthermore, Greaves (2019) state that this fragmentation does not imply
that more conflict will come from within the region but will most likely
be caused by external factors spilling into the region. As a result, the
Arctic will become less distinct and more influenced by global factors,
reflecting the dynamics of neighboring regions rather than its unique
characteristics.

However, the perspective of Arctic exceptionalism is still relevant.
Arctic exceptionalism recognizes the distinct vulnerabilities of the
Arctic due to its extreme environmental conditions, which cannot be
easily compared to other regions like the Atlantic or Pacific Oceans.
Arctic exceptionalism underscores the critical importance of addressing
the region\'s specific environmental and security issues, such as rapid
climate change and the need for sustainable development. Furthermore,
supporters of Arctic exceptionalism might argue that [even if there is
increasing interaction with neighboring regions]{.underline}, [this does
not **diminish** the Arctic\'s uniqueness]{.underline}. Instead, [it
highlights the need for **international cooperation** and **governance
structures** tailored to the Arctic\'s distinct
characteristics]{.underline}.

Moreover, as the case of the melting Arctic has illustrated, the Arctic
faces many intricate security challenges. However, Käpylä and Mikkola
(2015) argue against the concept of Arctic exceptionalism, which
portrays the Arctic as a uniquely isolated and peaceful region. They
contend that this view is overly limited, asserting that the
contemporary Arctic is deeply entwined with global political dynamics
and should not be perceived in isolation. They substantiate their
argument with empirical cases, such as the Arctic Sunrise protest and
the Ukraine crisis, illustrating the Arctic\'s susceptibility to
external influences, challenging the notion of inherent regional
tranquility. Moreover, the authors emphasize the shortcomings of Arctic
governance mechanisms when dealing with complex political scenarios and
underscore the historical precedent of international conflicts spilling
over into the Arctic. This argument has credibility from how the AC
cooperation froze as a result of Russia's invasion of Ukraine.

While Käpylä and Mikkola (2015) make a compelling case for the Arctic\'s
interconnectedness with global dynamics, proponents of Arctic
exceptionalism argue that these global linkages do not negate the
region\'s unique characteristics and cooperative nature. They contend
that [while external factors can influence Arctic affairs]{.underline},
[the region has historically exhibited a remarkable degree of
**stability** and **cooperation** among Arctic states]{.underline},
[**despite** global conflicts]{.underline}. Arctic exceptionalism
proponents point to the AC as a forum where Arctic nations engage in
collaborative efforts on various issues, emphasizing the Arctic\'s
capacity for peaceful multilateral governance. They argue that [while
the Arctic may not be **completely immune** to global
influences]{.underline}, [its distinct]{.underline} geographical,
environmental, and geopolitical [features continue to foster
**cooperation** and **dialogue** among its stakeholders]{.underline},
[**setting it apart** from more conflict- prone areas of the
world]{.underline}.

Furthermore, the lens of Arctic exceptionalism offers a unique
perspective through which to understand the intricate dynamics between
the Arctic's distinctive vulnerabilities and the realm of great power
politics. As previously discussed, the Arctic region stands as a beacon
of rapid environmental transformation, prominently featuring the
emergence of previously inaccessible resources and newly established
shipping routes as primary drivers of these profound shifts (Foreign
Policy, 2020; Brodka, 2021; Marsh, 2022). This intersection of
environmental metamorphosis and the pursuit of geopolitical interests
within the Arctic stage underscores the exceptional and nuanced nature
of the Arctic's response to the world's broader challenges. Lackenbauer
on the other hand, argue that the Arctic region is not a region of
exceptional cooperation nor conflict as nations interests can have both
a cooperative and competitive nature Teeple, 2021). This is exemplified
in the Arctic states' cooperation through the AC, simultaneously as
countries such as the US and Russia participates in geopolitical
competition in the Arctic.

The intense race to exploit the untapped Arctic resources, including
oil, gas, and minerals, has, in turn, amplified the tensions among great
powers as Russia, China, and the United States vie for dominance in this
fragile environment (Bordka, 2021; Gross, 2020; Lopez, 2021; Humpert,
2022). However, the scope of this competition extends beyond traditional
resource rivalry, delving into previously uncharted territories. For
instance, the revelation of valuable minerals in Greenland, now
accessible due to receding ice, holds immense significance for green
technologies, thereby attracting mining companies (Marsh, 2022). This
multifaceted contestation reflects the intricate balance that Arctic
exceptionalism illuminates---a delicate balance between economic
interests and environmental concerns unparalleled in any other context.
Furthermore, Young et al. (2021) state that it is one cannot ignore the
Arctic as a stage for future global competition, but rather provides the
perspective of the Arctic as a zone for peaceful competition.

Moreover, Arctic exceptionalism does not merely underscore [the
**environmental repercussions** of resource extraction and
infrastructure development in the Arctic]{.underline}; it also
[emphasizes the **unparalleled vulnerabilities** inherent to the
region]{.underline}. For instance, [the expansion of **oil drilling** in
this unique ecosystem poses not just risks but **dire
consequences**]{.underline}, [such as the potential for oil spills that
could inflict **devastating harm** upon the fragile Arctic marine
ecosystem]{.underline} (Borshchevskaia et al., 2022). Furthermore, [the
opening of Arctic **shipping** routes]{.underline}, while promising
economic gains, [ushers in the peril of increased maritime traffic that
introduces **noise pollution**]{.underline}---a disruption that imperils
Arctic marine mammals' communication and navigation (WWF Arctic,
n.d.-a). These instances underscore the extraordinary character of the
Arctic's vulnerabilities.

[Within this complex tapestry of challenges lies the predominant
necessity of **responsible governance**]{.underline}, firmly positioning
itself as the core of Arctic exceptionalism's application. [In the heart
of great power politics]{.underline}, [the AC is a **cooperative
platform** where states convene to collectively address the multifaceted
environmental and human security concerns]{.underline}, [even as
geopolitical rivalries play out]{.underline}. Arctic exceptionalism
recognizes the pivotal role of sustainable development in navigating
this ever-accelerating pace of Arctic transformation. [Responsible
governance would emerge as an **imperative** to forestall environmental
degradation and safeguard]{.underline} Arctic communities'
[well-being]{.underline}.

Resource [competition]{.underline}, as manifested [in the
Arctic]{.underline}, does more than reshape the region; it [transforms
it into an intricate theater where strategic calculations]{.underline},
[environmental imperatives]{.underline}, [and geopolitical rivalries
converge]{.underline}. Within this dynamic tapestry, [striking a
**balance** between economic interests]{.underline}, [environmental
preservation]{.underline}, [and human security becomes an
**extraordinary challenge**]{.underline}, uniquely expounded upon by the
framework of Arctic exceptionalism. [It underscores the **profound
importance** of]{.underline} prioritizing the Arctic's inhabitants'
welfare and the global environment's well-being in pursuing the region's
resource wealth. In navigating these labyrinthine intersections, great
powers could exemplify [leadership in addressing the **global
challenges** that transcend the Arctic while embracing the tenets of
**responsible governance** and **international
cooperation**]{.underline} within the exceptional Arctic context.

### Framework Cards---2NC

#### Debate is not just a game, but a [research apparatus]{.underline} in which debaters, speeches, and research are all [fundamentally entangled]{.underline} with the broader political context. The role of debaters and judges is to be [intra-actors]{.underline} who take ethical responsibility for [how]{.underline} we research and represent security.

**Murphy, 22**---Banting Postdoctoral Fellow in the Department of
Political Studies at Queen's University (Michael, "Entangled observers?
A quantum perspective on authority in critical security studies,"
Critical Studies on Security, 10(3), 119--131, dml)

If the [research]{.underline} apparatus is not defined by its laboratory
instrumentation, then what is it? Well, for Barad, '[apparatuses are
specific material configurations]{.underline}, or rather, dynamic
(re)configurings of the world [through which bodies are **intra-actively
materialized**]{.underline} ... [apparatuses are not bounded objects or
structures]{.underline}; [they are open-ended practices]{.underline}'
(Barad Citation2007, 169--170). If [the apparatus is **fundamentally
entangled** with the matter under observation in a broader
phenomenon]{.underline}, then [the]{.underline} former [idea of
ontological objectivity **evaporates**]{.underline} with the notion of
definitive borders and boundaries. In its place we find a new agential
kind of objectivity, where what defines [the research
apparatus]{.underline} is that 'the apparatus [enacts an **agential
cut**]{.underline} ... [within the phenomenon]{.underline}' (Barad
Citation2007, 175). While Barad's new materialist commitments recognise
the agency of nonhuman actants, their key argument about the
experimental apparatus is that [the decision to **observe** should be
understood as an act of **agency** within a broader
context]{.underline}. [As researcher]{.underline}, [I am an
**intra-actor**]{.underline} Footnote5 [exercising agency within a
system and participating in the construction of measurements and
findings]{.underline}. [I am **responsible** and
**accountable**]{.underline} (Barad Citation2007, 382) [for the
**entanglements** and **collapses** that I help enact with the research
apparatus and social context]{.underline}. Because the researcher,
research apparatus and object of attention are all entangled with one
another and within a broader natural/cultural and social/material
phenomenon, [the researcher has an **ethical duty** to
intra-act]{.underline} responsibly in a manner that balances a respect
for nonhuman agency [without **deflecting** responsibility]{.underline}
onto other human or nonhuman entanglements (Barad Citation2007, 391).
Through the process of becoming researcher, I emerge from the
pre-existing relation of observation, and [my entanglement within this
system entails an **ethical responsibility** to the **full description**
of my]{.underline} social and experimental [positionality within
it]{.underline}.Footnote6

In addition to Barad's exposition of entanglement as it relates to the
ethical stakes of transparency in research as a natural/cultural and
social/material process, we find a more directly political reflection in
Patricia Noxolo's quantum-inspired work on entanglement in social,
spatiotemporal and emancipatory contexts. In her application of quantum
ideas to cultural analysis, entanglement (and superposition) provides a
more direct expression of the postcolonial and postdiasporic presents to
explain and futures to explore. Similar to the work of Karen Barad, we
encounter in Noxolo's work a quantum social theory that is dynamic,
relational and agential:

Just as the post- of postcolonial is about living with a continued
entanglement with the colonial, the post- of postdiaspora is about
living with the continued consequences of the diasporic move, within the
postcolonial conditions that have motivated a wider range of movements
to former colonial centres, and which determine the conditions of
settlement. Postdiaspora denotes a legacy of diaspora but also of the
colonial, and neither is yet settled. (Noxolo Citation2020, 137)

The colonial, like the diaspora, remains entangled with the social
system of its subsequent post- form, and like any entangled system, the
latter cannot be fully understood independently. While connectedness
need not always be framed in quantum terms, the non-locality of
spatio-temporality in Noxolo's social theory clearly connects quantum
ideas with the postcolonial and decolonial. While the Newtonian
assumptions of colonial politics (see Bowman Citation2021) are rife with
local-realist reductionism, a quantum postcoloniality radically rejects
this fixed spatial scale: 'reality is not actually determined at the
local scale: [each local reality is **inextricably linked** with very
many elsewheres]{.underline}' (Noxolo Citation2020, 136). [Transversal
connections bind]{.underline} homeland, hostland and global family
[relations **far exceeding** the local]{.underline} realism of Newtonian
social science. Similarly, Noxolo frames the temporal connectivity as a
temporally nonlocal entanglement with colonial violences and histories.
'There is a very intimate, entangled affect around place and identity in
indigenous and decolonial theories,' she says, which 'starts from the
embodied self in place, and the violences done to the body, to place, as
well as to the entangled relations between them' (Noxolo Citation2018,
803). Taking this political dimension of Noxolo's project seriously, we
come to realise that quantum social theory supports decolonial and
postcolonial politics both ontologically (insofar as its relationality
gives voice to colonial, diasporic and other relations ontologically
erased by Newtonian separability) and epistemologically (insofar as the
very possibility of a quantum social theory deflates the universalising
claim to knowledge found within the tacitly Newtonian social science).

But there is something even more fascinating to be found here, at least
in the context of [this]{.underline} special issue's theme on authorship
and authority is concerned, because Noxolo's account [emphasises the
creative authority of written expression in **forwarding political
goals**]{.underline}. [It is by]{.underline} creative
[expression]{.underline} -- the authority [of activist and academic
alike]{.underline} -- through a quantum worldview [that we can
contribute to the **broader emancipatory politics** of
critique]{.underline}.Footnote7 [Language serves as the **medium** for
non-local community-building and writing can thereby **directly
support** the pursuit of postcolonial political projects]{.underline}
(Noxolo and Preziuso Citation2012, 133). Furthermore, 'decolonial
writing ... reflects very acutely on these body-place entanglements, and
the epistemic questions they raise in and for academic work' (Noxolo
Citation2018, 803). Just as Barad calls for the rejection of a binary
between nature and culture, Noxolo similarly draws on quantum notions of
entanglement to express the connectivity of body and place. We find,
also, meaningful resonance of this account of decolonial writing within
Zanotti's (Citation2018, Citation2021) quantum IR, where agents are
called to recognise their embeddedness in material/cultural phenomena
and the radical, relational responsibility entailed by that
entanglement.Footnote8 Finally, I would like to suggest that Karen
O'Brien's (Citation2021) account of transformational change becoming
possible through a recognition of our entanglements helps us to
understand the agential dimension of this decolonial project. While
O'Brien's empowering call that '**[you matter more than you
think]{.underline}**' emerges in the context of an agent facing the
existential threat of climate change and certainly resonates there, we
find a new emancipation when we read O'Brien and Noxolo in a diffracted
manner: decolonial quantum social theory asserts that the marginalised,
colonised and displaced peoples matter; that their places in the world
matter; and that their actions matter. Let us not underplay the
emancipatory significance of this quantum move.

Critical security studies and entangled observation: three insights on
authorship

[The ways we **imagine** how the world is made **shapes** what we
consider **politically possible**]{.underline}. [Knowledge and how we
validate it are **political acts**]{.underline}. Quantum ontological
imaginary repositions us vis-à-vis the universe. In engaging the world
as agents, [we do not act as **monadic**]{.underline}, perpetually
[**divided** subjects endowed with a reason capable of **understanding
God's plan**]{.underline} and elevating us closer to him, [but as
**entangled** phenomena that are part of what-is]{.underline}. We do not
act upon the world. Instead, we have agency as part of it. (Zanotti
Citation2021, 458)

Quantum IR is not the first new imaginary to be proposed in critical
International Relations (e.g. Cohn Citation1987), though its particular
path is novel. The new research community at the nexus of critical and
quantum approaches describe the quantum world as offering a novel
imaginary that challenges the positionality of the human, the
researcher, and -- most importantly for the topic of this special issue
-- the author vis-à-vis the social world, if not the entire universe
(Zanotti Citation2018; Murphy Citation2021). [The inseparability of
observers from their social relations]{.underline}, [the
experiment]{.underline}, [and the observations **challenges
fundamentally** the positivist imaginary]{.underline} grounding much of
mainstream social sciences. To this end, quantum social theory's lessons
for epistemology, methodology, ethics and politics should come as no
surprise to many critical security studies scholars -- but that, as they
say, is a feature rather than a bug. Many of the arguments voiced in
earlier moments of critical security studies (such as the broadening and
deepening debate and the methods turn) are triangulated from the first
principles of a quantum perspective. Therefore, in addition to providing
a common language to describe the entanglement of the observer to the
observed and the author to the text, there is also a freedom from
Newtonian/positivist constraints that must first be fought back against
in the earlier efforts within critical theory. This section discusses
three conceptual intersections of critical and quantum thought to help
address the question, 'What is an Author?'

Full description of the apparatus includes the observerFootnote9

[Authorship is an **essential** part of the research
process]{.underline}, just [as the designer of the apparatus becomes
**entangled** with its construction through the choices
made]{.underline} and the observer effect entangles the observer with
the ontological (and/or epistemological, depending upon the
interpretation) status of the wavefunction. This is a concern expressed
well in the language of the methods turn, from Mutlu's repudiation of
sanitised methods sections that erase researcher's footsteps to
Leander's description of the embeddedness and embodiment of ethnographic
observation. The conceptual discussion of Bohr and empirical discussion
of the Stern--Gerlach experiment provide a clear argument from a quantum
perspective for the necessity of including the researcher in the
description of the experiment. And, given that the decisions continue
far into the process of writing, [entanglement of the researcher with
knowledge-production continues well into the stage of
authorship]{.underline}.

The social construction of knowledge extends into its social context

Barad's deconstruction of the Stern--Gerlach apparatus points out how
the conditions of possibility for the production of silver sulphide rely
not merely on the materiality of a particular cigar, but also on the
cultural influences of gender, class, social norms and myriad other
factors that influence the scholar's positionality in approaching the
research process. Like Stern's cheap cigar, [we may not
**expect**]{.underline} ([and]{.underline} post-hoc, we [may **naively
ignore**]{.underline}) [the importance of social factors in contouring
the **conditions of possibility** for the knowledge production in which
we participate]{.underline}, [but recognising the entanglement of
society into research is **paramount** to ensuring]{.underline} that the
[full description]{.underline} proposed so many decades ago by Bohr can
be ensured. Here, too, we uncover the utility of quantum concepts, as
Noxolo's use of entanglement to expresses identity, continued historical
influences and relationality in postcolonial, postdiasporic and
Indigenous theories.

Authorship enacts agential cuts into the broader socio-material context

[The broader **political implications** of scholarship are]{.underline}
already [documented in the]{.underline} broadening debate and methods
turn, with the Aberystwyth school arguing for the [**inherent political
stakes** of defining **security**]{.underline} and Mutlu highlighting
the emancipatory power of openly shared knowledge. Quantum transforms
this conversation both by expanding the scope of relevant agency while
also raising the ethical stakes of responsibility in becoming entangled
with the broader research context. The author does not have a monopoly
on agency in the research process (nor even authorship). Material
configurations of computers, servers and internet connections facilitate
the recording and sharing of the written word, while [social
constellations]{.underline} support research financially, incentivise
placement in prestigious venues and [**discipline debates** to privilege
**established** and **mainstream** perspectives]{.underline}. But [at
the same time]{.underline}, the agential cut that the author makes by
participating in the production of knowledge cannot be effaced by the
existence of agency beyond the self, and [the author bears an **ethical
responsibility** to their entanglements]{.underline}. While this may
seem to be a burden or a negative statement, Noxolo's discussion of the
ability of language to foster nonlocal community-building reveals that
ethical entanglement can be as emancipatory as it is daunting.

Conclusion

Approaching entanglement through language allows us to consider that
'**[meaningful]{.underline}**' [conversations can **influence** how we
**engage with social change**]{.underline}: [when we are **aware** that
we are entangled within]{.underline} a quantum system and part of [the
phenomena that we are measuring]{.underline}, [we have **greater
potential** to **open up inquiries** with others and **generate
insights** that are **truly transformative**]{.underline}. (O'Brien
Citation2021, 70)

In the end, then, what is an author [in critical security
studies]{.underline}? Inspired by quantum social theory, I argue that
[the critical author is an **entangled observer**]{.underline}, [an
**intra-actor** who recognises the agency that emerges from the
practices and processes of scholarship]{.underline} (beyond the self)
[and the **unerasable ethical responsibility** for the agential cuts
that the author helps to enact]{.underline}. [It is not only the
definition of **what counts as security** that has **ethico-political
implications** for authors and their contexts]{.underline}. [Nor
is]{.underline} the fulsome account of [methodology]{.underline} and
social positionality of research [relevant **solely** as a matter
of]{.underline} transparency, rigour, or [research ethics]{.underline}.
Quantum social theory suggests that [these considerations are **woven
together**]{.underline}, inseparably entangled and only fully
understandable in relation to the other. Quantum IR places the
broadening debate and the methods turn in superposition, challenging all
to recognise responsibility, transparency and agency in the production
of what Karen O'Brien calls truly transformative insights.

In an important way, this recognition of the ethical stakes of academic
creativity brings our conversation on authorship back to the common root
of authority. [When we take on the **authorial role**]{.underline}, [we
enact our authority in the word and in the world]{.underline}.
[Recognising this connectivity of word to world demands]{.underline}, in
turn, [the **acknowledgement** of our entanglement in our
surroundings]{.underline}. [This is true of our place in
**institutions**]{.underline}, [our connectedness to our **subject
matter**]{.underline}, [our embeddedness in the **research process** and
beyond]{.underline}. And, [recognising the **ethical imperatives** of
entanglement]{.underline} in quantum social theory (Barad Citation2007;
Zanotti Citation2018, Citation2021), it is precisely this recognition of
the fundamental connectivity between authorship/authority that [infuses
critique with a **transformational agency**]{.underline} (O'Brien
Citation2021). As scholarship continues to explore the quantum/critical
nexus, authors will help to enact a variety of agential cuts, embedded
and embodied in the research that they collaborate to produce.

#### Representations are [prescriptions]{.underline}, not just [descriptions]{.underline}. Excluding the consequences of our [discursive justifications]{.underline} from Arctic debates [internal link turns]{.underline} any benefit to their framework---it [distorts]{.underline} our research and [lowers]{.underline} the quality of your decisionmaking.

**Nabok, 22**---Senior Lecturer, Saint Petersburg State University
(Sergey, "Main Theoretical Approaches in the Arctic Policy Studies,"
Arctic and North, 2022, no. 47, pp. 119--135, dml)

In the scientific study of international relations, international
political processes and management systems, the role of theory is
characterized by two specific features that distinguish it from other
social disciplines. Firstly, the actual [political analysis used in the
study of specific issues]{.underline}, [such as]{.underline} state
**[policy]{.underline}**, inter-state relations, or international
institutions, [**rarely** relies on a **well-defined** set of concepts
and propositions that establish key relationships between the phenomena
under study]{.underline}, [is **ad hoc** in nature]{.underline}, [and
claims to **generalize** conclusions]{.underline}. Although theory is an
indispensable element of scientific discourse, its factual usefulness in
political analysis is insignificant, and [theoretical
provisions]{.underline} (which are hypotheses in the epistemological
sense) [are often accepted as **implicit suppositions**]{.underline},
[**without** reflection and critical analysis]{.underline}.

Secondly, as F. Chernoff notes, [the **prescriptive** component is
**extremely important**]{.underline} in the theory of international
relations: [although some theories seek to describe only the
interrelations of phenomena]{.underline}, [theoretical analysis often
goes **beyond** a strict description and **explicitly** or
**implicitly** formulates certain political goals and **normative
criteria** used to **evaluate** a]{.underline} certain actual or
**[hypothetical course of action]{.underline}** \[1, p. 3--4\]. The
latter leads to the fact that political [theory acts as a **discursive
justification** of a certain political model and as a **basis** for a
political narrative that **legitimizes** a certain course of
action]{.underline}.

Let us [consider]{.underline}, for example, a simple theoretical
statement, corresponding to the position of [the **realist**
paradigm]{.underline}: "[The **only** type of actors whose actions
**matter** for]{.underline} [international politics and]{.underline}
international [relations are **states**]{.underline}". [Taking this
statement as an **implicit** assumption focuses political analysis
**exclusively** on the actions and decisions taken by the highest levels
of state power]{.underline}, without even allowing the question of the
possible role of non-state actors. [If such political analysis is faced
with the need to explain the behavior of **non-state**
actors]{.underline} (for example, NPOs), [they will be interpreted
either as **insignificant** or as **instrumental**]{.underline}, that
is, as [**tools** of state actors' decisions]{.underline}. [If this
theoretical premise becomes the **basis for
policy-making**]{.underline}, [the consequence is that **any meaningful
processes** in]{.underline} **[i]{.underline}**nternational
**[r]{.underline}**elations [have to be interpreted as resulting from
the decisions of **state** actors]{.underline}. Any actions of non-state
actors are construed as being inspired by the governments of other
countries, and the actors themselves are deprived of subjectivity,
including in a practical political sense.

[Such **uncritical acceptance** of theoretical propositions and the
**failure to distinguish** between descriptive and prescriptive
elements]{.underline} of theory [have **important negative
consequences** for both]{.underline} the scientific
**[study]{.underline}** of political processes [and political
**practice**]{.underline}. In a practical sense, [this **incorrectly
evaluates** information]{.underline}, [makes **erroneous
decisions**]{.underline}, [and **narrows the range** of available
behavioral strategies]{.underline}. In analytical terms, [the focus of
attention is **distorted** and the risk of **misinterpretation** of the
observed phenomena **increases**]{.underline}. For example, the
systematic efforts of China to influence the regional policy in the
United States \[2, de La Bruyère D., Pikarsic N.\] in case of uncritical
acceptance of the postulate of the state as the only actor in the world
political arena should be recognized as meaningless and not having
rational justification.

[These problems]{.underline} of the theory of international relations
[are]{.underline} also [typical for the study of the
**Arctic**]{.underline}. The [authors of publications
citing]{.underline} the results of [empirical research and political
analysis do not always rely on **clearly formulated** theoretical
positions and analytical models]{.underline}, [and the descriptive
characteristics of the subject under study]{.underline} (for example,
the Arctic strategy of a particular state) [are **explicitly** or
**implicitly** based on the adoption of certain political
**goals**]{.underline}, in according to which the situation and
decisions are evaluated. At the same time, [**explicitly formulated**
theoretical provisions play an **important role** both in]{.underline}
scientific [**research** and in formulation of **practical
recommendations**]{.underline}. [Theory performs several important
functions]{.underline}: [defines the **focus** of research and the
**specific subjects** to be studied and analyzed]{.underline};
[establishes the **content** and **relationships** between key variables
used for explanation and prediction]{.underline}; [allows to **evaluate
alternative mechanisms** for achieving political goals]{.underline}
(without a priori determination of these goals).

## Links

### Link---Adaptation, Resilience, Sensing, Etc---2NC

#### The 1AC securitizes the Arctic as a region of "unknown unknowns" that can only be adapted to by biopolitical expansion of borders and sensing architectures.

**Johnson, 24**---Assistant Professor of Humane Artificial Intelligence
and International Relations in the department of International Relations
and International Organizations at the University of Groningen
(Benjamin, "This world of tomorrow: sociotechnical imaginaries of
security in the Canadian Arctic," Critical Studies on Security, 12(2),
187--208, dml)

[From a security perspective]{.underline}, [**climate change** is
presented as a **threat multiplier**]{.underline} in a quantitative
sense [as it increases the scale of potentialities for the Arctic's
exploitation]{.underline} against Canada's interests and sovereignty.
Building on the New Arctic's ontological foundation as a sociotechnical
imaginary, [maritime **surveillance** of these actors and the expected
increase in Arctic traffic is of]{.underline} particular
[concern]{.underline} for Canada. From a defence standpoint, [one of the
**critical challenges** currently facing the successful monitoring of
this traffic is the lack of diversity in the **number** and **types** of
sensors in the region]{.underline}. The automatic identification system
(AIS) for monitoring marine vessels, coupled with radar and satellite
linkage (Satlink), can monitor vessels that conform to broadcasting an
AIS signature. However, there are several gaps in current surveillance
capabilities. [Within this framework]{.underline}, '**[dark
targets]{.underline}**' [constitute the **most urgent**
threat]{.underline} to the DND and CAF in the region because they 'are
not picked up on sensors or cannot be identified' (Lackenbauer and
Kikkert Citation2018, 25).

Consequently, [defence specialists and practitioners argue that there is
a clear need to develop **new technologies** and **methods** to locate
and identify these objects]{.underline}, including combining sensor
platforms through a system of systems approach into a 'multi-sensor
maritime domain awareness picture' (Lackenbauer and Kikkert
Citation2018, 25). [The threats associated with]{.underline} '**[unknown
unknowns]{.underline}**' [invoke a methodological need for **mapping**
behaviours in terms of an actor's]{.underline} '**[patterns of
life]{.underline}**' when individual events are disparate, unremarkable,
and difficult to link with one another. This behaviour exists below the
threshold of threat activity [that]{.underline} may be otherwise treated
as discrete and unimportant. However, this series of behaviours [may
demonstrate an **explicit security threat**]{.underline} when analysed
holistically. An example presented in the proceedings of a defence
roundtable on implementing Canada's Arctic priorities under SSE
illustrates this securitised logic: '[a small **fishing vessel** could
be carrying a **cruise missile** or bringing in illegal **foreign
fighters**]{.underline}' (Lackenbauer and Kikkert Citation2018, 25).
[The potential infiltration of overt security threats via these dark
targets represents a **Trojan Horse** analogue made more dangerous
through its **decentralisation** into networked
sub-components]{.underline}, which can infiltrate the state's sovereign
space at different nodal points. Individual events may appear
unexceptional when viewed independently but become more concerning from
a risk-based perspective when examined holistically and relationally.

Strategically, [extending **border** practices]{.underline} is crucial
to achieving Canada's Arctic goals identified within S.S.E. Lackenbauer
and Kikkert state that Canada needs to be 'pushing the borders outward'
and that '\[d\]efence, safety, and security requirements dictate that
Canada and its allies must detect, track, and identify vessels when they
leave a foreign port and operate in Canada's \[extended economic zone\],
not just when they enter into Canada's territorial sea (12 nautical
miles from the cost or our straight baselines)' (Citation2018, 21, 24).
The notion of pushing borders outward [highlights the dual process of
rationalisation by the state towards **sovereign control** over its
northern boundaries]{.underline}. DRDC emphasises a similar theme and
the role of technological development in their S&T strategy, which
states that 'the CAF also require additional time and space to react to
threats, for example by extending sensor coverage as far away as
possible from our forces and leveraging automation and stand-off
capacity of CAF weapon systems' (National Defence Citation2014, 15, my
emphasis).In particular, 'as globalization erased traditional concepts
of time and space, making borders porous and encouraging continental
integration, national sovereignty was reshaped and the power of national
governments to control events reduced' (Dean, Lackenbauer, and
Lajeunesse Citation2014, 24 my emphasis). [The **growing porousness** of
borders renders states **vulnerable** to many emerging threats that are
increasingly]{.underline} international, if not
**[global]{.underline}**, [in their orientation]{.underline}.

[Pushing sovereignty **outward**]{.underline}, therefore,
[embodies]{.underline} both spatial and temporal characteristics via
[technologically mediated practices of **surveillance** and
**intelligence**]{.underline}, [which are combined to form techniques of
**sensing**]{.underline}. [Sensing is a **crucial** state-led technique
of **sovereign power** for providing **situational
awareness**]{.underline} (SA) [that]{.underline} '[provides]{.underline}
the Government with [the ability to perceive the physical]{.underline}
(maritime, land, air, and space) [and non-physical]{.underline} (cyber
and human) [domains]{.underline}' while 'allow\[ing\] for the fusion,
evaluation, and dissemination of that information' [to
create]{.underline} '**[intelligence]{.underline}**' (Government of
Canada Citation2010, 30). In brief, [sensing is **acutely biopolitical**
in categorising]{.underline}, [filtering]{.underline}, [and reassembling
surveillance data from multiple sensors and deploying that reorganised
data as intelligence for **state action**]{.underline}. Thus,
[technology-enabled sensing is envisioned as a **core** feature of
**expanding** and **consolidating** the state's sovereign power in the
Arctic]{.underline}.

[Projecting security through a risk framework socially creates the
potential for a **much more comprehensive** range and volume of threats
in the form of potentialities]{.underline} (Amoore Citation2013; Coker
Citation2009).While Beck famously defines the risk society as one that
is socially 'preoccupied \[with\] the future' (Beck Citation1992, 21),
the increasing salience of risk as a framing mechanism for security
practices is a more recent thematic turn and signals the influence of
actuarial management and insurance schematics on security (Lobo-Guerrero
Citation2011). [These frameworks are specifically concerned with the
**unknown unknowns** of the more distant future]{.underline},
[especially as the Arctic's melting ice may lead to challenges
for]{.underline} Canada's **[sovereignty]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} de facto control over its **[territorial
borders]{.underline}** and adjacent waters (Grant Citation2016, 30;
Lackenbauer Citation2011, 80).

### Link---Arctic Science, Innovation, Tech, Etc---2NC

#### The 1AC generates [new vectors]{.underline} of Arctic securitization by demanding [immediate]{.underline} scientific and technological responses to complex future threats. 

**Johnson, 24**---Assistant Professor of Humane Artificial Intelligence
and International Relations in the department of International Relations
and International Organizations at the University of Groningen
(Benjamin, "This world of tomorrow: sociotechnical imaginaries of
security in the Canadian Arctic," Critical Studies on Security, 12(2),
187--208, dml)

[Arctic security in the modern era is **bound** with **futurity**
because threats that undermine the state's sovereignty are considered
**potentialities** rather than clear and present dangers]{.underline}.
While the term 'threat' already implies what may happen rather than what
will, [the nature of those threats as potentialities is spatially and
temporally **expanded**]{.underline}. [The risks associated with threats
**increase** as threats are **projected farther** into the
future]{.underline}, [especially concerning state
sovereignty]{.underline}, [which offers a sense of **immediacy** to the
Arctic's security in the present]{.underline}. For instance, former
Prime Minister Stephen Harper drew explicitly from this Arctic imaginary
by insisting that, in an often-cited passage, Canadians in the 22nd --
century demand action in the 21st and that Canada was at risk of
'losing' the Arctic if it did not 'use' it (Dodds Citation2012, 994).

[There is a consistent thread that **permeates** the various **Arctic
imaginaries** over time]{.underline}, [from efforts towards **colonial
expansion**]{.underline} that rested on the themes of adventure,
mystery, and taming the Arctic's wildness as a frontier space [through
the mastery of **marine navigation** to the equally colonised
imaginaries of **northward expansion** in service of]{.underline}
Canada's **[nation-building]{.underline}** efforts [and the defence of
**sovereignty**]{.underline} throughout the Cold War and now. In all
cases, [the axes of sovereign control have rested **principally** on
notions of **scientific progress** and **technological
innovation**]{.underline}. [Science and technology are **critical
vectors** of modernisation as an **idealised practice**]{.underline},
[which have **consistently underpinned** political efforts by the state
to **rationalise** and **control** the Arctic]{.underline}. Thus, [the
**goal**]{.underline} of rationalising the Arctic's spatial and temporal
vectors [is not new]{.underline}. [However]{.underline}, the specific
characteristic of networked complexity and [the **intensity** of
the]{.underline} spatial and temporal [components]{.underline} (i.e. the
degree to which time and space are collapsed as impediments to accessing
and controlling the Arctic) [underlying the New Arctic implies a
**novelty** in how the Arctic is understood concerning this complexity
and how it should be secured]{.underline}.

### Link---China, Russia---2NC\*

#### \*Note while prepping: Consider highlighting different versions of this card depending on whether the aff talks about Russia, China, or both!

#### Their depictions of \[Chinese/Russian\] threats in the Arctic are not neutral---they reflect American [status anxiety]{.underline} that empirically triggers [aggressive militarization]{.underline}.

**Fakhoury, 25**---Ph.D. candidate in Global Studies, University of
Massachusetts Lowell (Renato, "Peoples, States, and the Arctic: Identity
and Narratives in Arctic Security Dynamics,"
<https://www.proquest.com/docview/3216782700>, dml)

Traditionally, the United States is not considered a status-anxious
state, since it possesses primacy over most hierarchies in the
international system. Diverse composite indicators8 rank the U.S. as
number 1 in regard to its military and capabilities, far from
runner-ups, and its prestige - although an abstract concept - helped
build and maintain the world order for the last century. American
positioning in the Arctic, however, is more complex than that. [The
strategic significance of the Arctic as a frontier has **increased** due
to the region's receding ice]{.underline}, [opening up new sea routes
and potential for resource extraction]{.underline}. [This shift has made
the Arctic a **focal point** for geopolitical competition]{.underline}.
[The U.S. has been **slow** to respond]{.underline}, [especially
compared to **Russia**]{.underline}, [which has significantly advanced
its capabilities in the region]{.underline}. Russia's extensive
icebreaker fleet, robust military presence, and infrastructural
developments have positioned it as the leading power in the Arctic.
**[China]{.underline}**, too, [has made substantial
investments]{.underline}, viewing the Arctic as a critical area for
expanding its influence. **[Status anxiety]{.underline}**, in this
context, [refers to the **fear** of further losing **global dominance**
and **influence**]{.underline}. [For decades]{.underline},
[the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates [has
been accustomed to a position of **unparalleled** geopolitical
power]{.underline}, [but the Arctic presents new challenges that
**threaten** this status]{.underline}.

[**Congressional hearings** on Arctic security **repeatedly highlight**
the anxiety underlying U.S. Arctic policy]{.underline}, [particularly
the fear of **falling behind** key competitors]{.underline}. [This sense
of **urgency**]{.underline}, largely absent before the 2010s, [has
become **increasingly pronounced** as lawmakers call for a **more robust
U.S. presence** to counteract the strategic advances of Russia and
China]{.underline}. [Records **consistently emphasize** the stark
disparity in capabilities]{.underline}, such as Russia's fleet of over
30 icebreakers compared to the United States' significantly smaller and
less capable fleet, exemplifying a critical gap in Arctic readiness.

[This anxiety is further reflected in U.S. **Arctic Strategy
documents**]{.underline} from 1994 to 2024, [which reveal a **dramatic
increase** in references to Russia]{.underline}, [China]{.underline},
[security]{.underline}, [and threats]{.underline}. Recent strategies
underscore the Arctic's transformation into a contested geopolitical
arena, [calling for enhanced U.S. capabilities and a heightened presence
in response to intensifying competition]{.underline}. The shift from
viewing the Arctic as a low-priority region to acknowledging its
critical importance [illustrates how anxiety about losing status and
influence has **driven a recalibration** of U.S. policy]{.underline}.
[These documents signal a recognition that the Arctic is]{.underline} no
longer a peripheral concern but [a **key battleground** for great power
rivalry]{.underline}, [necessitating a **more militarized** and
**proactive** approach to protect American interests and sustain
geopolitical standing]{.underline}.

Historically, the U.S.'s passive Arctic stance stemmed from its
strategic focus on other regions and a perception of the Arctic as less
immediately critical. However, the aggressive expansion of Russian and
Chinese investments and activities in the region has forced a
reevaluation. Now [faced with catching up in a region where its
competitors have gained significant footholds]{.underline}, [the U.S.
has embraced a **more assertive** Arctic policy]{.underline}. [This
shift exemplifies how status anxiety]{.underline}, [fueled by the rapid
advancements of rivals]{.underline}, [can **catalyze strategic
action**]{.underline}. [Congressional hearings and policy documents
**increasingly reflect** this anxiety]{.underline}, underscoring the
pressing need for the U.S. to strengthen its Arctic capabilities to
safeguard its geopolitical interests and maintain its global standing.

Conclusion

In this paper, I analyze how [the evolution of U.S. Arctic policy
reflects a **broader narrative** of status anxiety]{.underline}, [which
has served]{.underline} not as a paralyzing force but [as a **catalyst**
for **strategic recalibration**]{.underline}. Historically, the Arctic
was peripheral to U.S. foreign policy, viewed primarily through the
domestic lens of Alaska and rarely integrated into the country's global
strategy. However, [the changing geopolitical
landscape]{.underline}---marked by the rise of Russia and China as key
players in the region---[has **redefined** the Arctic as a critical
frontier for great power competition]{.underline}. The [analysis of U.S.
Arctic policy]{.underline} documents and Congressional hearings [reveals
a **pattern**]{.underline}: [the **increasing securitization** of Arctic
discourse aligns with a growing recognition of the region's strategic
importance]{.underline}. [Anxiety about losing influence in the Arctic
has propelled the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates [toward **more proactive**
measures]{.underline}, [including alliance-building]{.underline},
