the constructivist 'account of identity' can offer. Identities can
furthermore contribute to the production of a state's actions, both
domestically and internationally, as they are potentially partaking in
shaping the state's constitutive practices (Agius 2019: 85; Hopf 1998:
186--187, 193). Cho (2012) emphasises the same thing by stating that
[the changeable identity and interests of a state lays the
**foundation** for its actions]{.underline} (Cho 2012: 305). [A state's
identity also relates to]{.underline} so called role identity and rights
and behavioural norms which are based on [how the state is
**recognised** by other states]{.underline} (Wendt 1999: 228), where
"enemy" and "friend" are two prominent examples of role identity. Wendt
(1999) argues that when defining those roles, the most significant
factor to consider is the interdependence, or so called "intimacy"
between the Other and the Self. When the intimacy is high between the
Self and the Other, states can be unable to define their own roles as a
certain position might be forced upon them by the representation of
Others, Others that might do so in order to ensure that their own
identity is ensured. As this occurs it can thus be difficult for states
to abandon the role that has been enforced upon them (Wendt 1999: 228).
Cho (2009) follows along the same line by stating that [when cultures
and norms create a collective identity]{.underline}, [this]{.underline}
collective in turn [**shape** state's **interests** as well as its
**policies**]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} that [it is a **must** that
the state act according to what its identity and interests
direct]{.underline} (Cho 2009: 81--82). Relating to this is the notion
of power, as conventional constructivists believe that [power
is]{.underline} to be [found **everywhere** since]{.underline} "[social
practices **reproduce** underlying power relations]{.underline}" (Hopf
1998: 185), and that many of the assets that are often attributed to
states such as egoism, the meaning of power and power-seeking, are all
in fact actually formed by the international system (Cho 2009: 88).

The concept of identity can further be connected enmity, which is the at
the end of the spectrum of roles regarding "relationships governing the
use of violence between Self and Other, distinct in kind from rivals and
friends" (Wendt 1999: 260). [By using this as a **starting point** in
understanding how the Other and the Self is represented]{.underline},
[one can **predict** and **explain** events within a certain social
system]{.underline}. An example of this is when the Others are
represented as an enemy, which means that they are not willing to limit
its violence against the Self since they do not consider that the Self
should have the rights to exist as an autonomous being. In a similar
fashion a rival opposes the Self by not recognising its rights to life
and liberty. [Depending on if the Other is an enemy or a
rival]{.underline}, [the level of violence one can expect from the Other
**changes**]{.underline}, [where violence between two enemies is only
limited by the]{.underline} so called 'inadequate capabilities' of the
states, which refers to exhaustion or a **[balance of
power]{.underline}** (Wendt 1999: 260--261). [The response of the states
is then built upon the concept of the Other and the perception of an
enemy]{.underline}, where they act to either conquer or destroy their
enemy. In those scenarios the decision-making process often put a strong
emphasis on worst case scenarios and negative possibilities, and as a
consequence of this tend to see military capabilities as crucial, which
makes it all the more unlikely to see cooperative moves from the enemy
(Wendt 1999: 262). Wendt thus argues that [realism gives an explanation
to power politics by using the perceptions of Other and Self as a
**reference point**]{.underline}, and furthermore that "[when the Other
is an enemy the Self is forced to **mirror back** the representations it
has contributed to the other]{.underline}" (Wendt 1999: 263).

When the Self is mirroring the Other, it is in other words becoming its
enemy in order to survive. In turn the hostile perceptions the Other had
about the Self will be confirmed, and as a result the Other will engage
in realpolitik as well. [This sequence of events in
realpolitik]{.underline} then [goes **round** and **round** and
can]{.underline} according to Wendt [be]{.underline} described as [a
**self-fulfilling prophecy**]{.underline} where the beliefs of an actor
"generate actions that confirms those beliefs" (Wendt 1999: 263), making
states existential threats by attributing hostile qualities to them
(ibid.). This means that states can help other states since it
identifies with it, or due to having a collective interest, which
relates to Wendt's statement about anarchy where he argues that an
[anarchy **does not necessarily** have to be a **self-helping** system
but could just as well be a **collective** security system]{.underline}
(Wendt 1994: 387--388, 240).

### AT: No Spillover To Broader Securitization

#### Arctic security imaginaries are a [testing ground]{.underline} for [global techniques]{.underline} of sovereign control.

**Johnson, 24**---Assistant Professor of Humane Artificial Intelligence
and International Relations in the department of International Relations
and International Organizations at the University of Groningen
(Benjamin, "This world of tomorrow: sociotechnical imaginaries of
security in the Canadian Arctic," Critical Studies on Security, 12(2),
187--208, dml)

Sociotechnical imaginaries are collective visions of the future
interlaced with notions of scientific progress and advancement towards
achieving or navigating that future. Sociotechnical imaginaries provide
a unique framing mechanism for understanding how the Arctic was
historically presented as a future frontier to be tamed through
scientific progress within a broader lens of economic expansion and
modernisation that populated the early 20th century and post-war vision.
The Cold War entailed a more overtly securitised imaginary, whereas the
21st-century Arctic entails different combinations of earlier
imaginaries within a contemporary field of expanded threats and
opportunities, ultimately culminating in the New Arctic. Others have
already defined [the **New Arctic**]{.underline}, but in this context,
it [is the sociotechnical expression of multiple **international** and
**global** trends underpinned by a particular interaction of
forces]{.underline}. Sociotechnical imaginaries involve ontological and
epistemological issues. State-led efforts towards enhancing sensing
abilities in the Arctic represent a specific epistemic approach to an
ontology of complexity, which resists easy and direct intervention. The
empirical material discussed in this paper is not exhaustive and should
not be seen as overly authoritative. However, as a whole, that material
demonstrates a consistent approach to understanding the contemporary
Arctic environment, and the interaction of efforts in the marine and
space domains illustrate this approach to Arctic security.

Thus, the Arctic imaginaries of the present echo some aspects of the
past. However, while the imaginary of the past portrayed the Arctic
frontier as a space that would ultimately be conquered through science
and technological superiority, the contemporary frontier dissolves in
the face of science and technology because it is those same forces that
undermine the Arctic's environmental integrity and expose it to
extra-sovereign interventions. As with any sociotechnical imaginary,
imagined futures often encounter the limits of technology, including its
socioeconomic valorisation in the world. The earlier sociotechnical
imaginary of a 'Canada of the North' never materialised for many
reasons, including the technical and economic realities of building
northern infrastructure. [It is within this wider sociotechnical lens
that sensing technologies **gain their currency** and
**form**]{.underline}, [especially as they are an **attractive policy
goal** that is malleable to multiple and possibly contradictory
sociotechnical imaginaries of the Arctic's future]{.underline}.

[Sociotechnical imaginaries thus amount to **more** than simple
**representations** and (re)produce relations of power that
**circumscribe** the dominant ontological foundations of the
world]{.underline}. Consequently, [the New Arctic is **shaping** forms
of **state intervention** and **governance**]{.underline}. This paper
has argued that the Arctic is a region considered within a relational
worldview premised on complexity and risk, which makes future threats
increasingly challenging to predict.Consequently, the New Arctic
requires techniques of sovereign power and governance that can make
sense of this environment. These techniques depend on technological
innovation related to advances in sensing capability because the sheer
complexity of this environment overwhelms human cognition.

This paper demonstrates how [the New Arctic is a sociotechnical
imaginary **shaping** the development of **sensing technologies**
designed for sovereign enforcement through]{.underline} the example of
[**space-based surveillance capabilities** and]{.underline} the use of
**[intelligence applications]{.underline}** like the BlueHawk product.
While [the Arctic represents a **unique space** where these technologies
are being **developed** and **deployed**]{.underline}, the proliferation
of sensing represents a much larger trend in geostrategic thinking.
However, [current efforts are **only possible** within the contemporary
security landscape's broader social and political context]{.underline}.
A recent evolution in that thinking flows from the Russian invasion of
Ukraine and involves the acceleration of debates on what it means for
Arctic governance. [This]{.underline} issue [speaks to the
**re-internationalisation** of Arctic security alongside its
globalisation]{.underline}, [intensifying]{.underline} the complexity
and intensity of those issues. Future research should engage with this
trend more substantially, particularly as Canada's focus on Arctic
sensing aligns with US and NORAD strategic thinking for continental
defence and as sensing technology intersects with other developments in
artificial intelligence and quantum. Overall, the cases discussed here
offer one example of [a much larger trend in **defence
thinking**]{.underline}, [and **technological development** centred on
integrating global sensor architectures to **pre-emptively secure** the
**Arctic** of today from the world of tomorrow]{.underline}.

### AT: Perm

#### Attempting to rehabilitate security remains locked within its terms\-\--attempting to redefine one example of a violent political order actively prevents resisting its very foundation

Chris **Rossdale 16**, Teaching Fellow, International Relations,
University of Warwick, "Activism, resistance and security," Chapter 14
of *Ethical Security Studies: A new research agenda*, eds. Nyman and
Burke, 2016, no page \#

The previous two sections have highlighted a number of ways in which
practices of resistance and activism engage the relationship between
ethics and security in different ways. [In]{.underline} producing
subjugated knowledges, revealing the exclusions and power relations of
established discourses, and [engaging in security practices which seek
to more directly respond to the insecurity faced by ordinary people,
they invite [an]{.mark} **[ethical response to security]{.mark}** and
insecurity]{.underline}. [However]{.underline}, [it [limits our
engagement]{.mark}]{.underline} with practices of resistance [[if
we]{.mark} only [see them as exploring]{.mark} 'better' or '[more
ethical' ways of]{.mark} [providing security]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[The
**more radical challenge**]{.underline}]{.mark} to the politics of
security [[comes]{.underline}]{.mark} when we see activism [[not simply
as refusing **particular orders** of security, but]{.mark} as [resisting
the **very**]{.mark} **conceptual and political [foundations of
security]{.mark}**]{.underline}. This final section explores such an
interpretation, looking at the ways in which the most substantive way to
engage the relationships between ethics, security, resistance and
activism comes when we view practices of resistance as (at their best)
working to deconstruct security. I begin by outlining some of the
arguments which suggest that the concept of **[[security cannot]{.mark}
so easily [be refashioned in a more ethical form]{.mark}]{.underline}**
and that thinking in terms of resistance might take us further. I then
look at how we might view such a resistance in the context of political
activism, looking at some examples from anarchist activist groups.

A number of writers have argued that the concept of [security is built
around a series of]{.underline} images, [codes and logics which render
it deeply problematic and a dangerous candidate for
rehabilitation]{.underline}. They have pointed out the ways in which
[our contemporary fascination with]{.underline} proliferating images of
[threat, danger and response]{.underline}, grounded in desperate but
impossible fantasies of control and mastery, [tends towards]{.underline}
authoritarian political formations and [the de facto legitimacy of
dominant power relations]{.underline} (Edkins 2003; Campbell 1998:
Shepherd 2008: 72--75). The pursuit of [[security serves to]{.mark}
**[contain subjects within the existing order]{.mark}**]{.underline},
promising protection in return for some level of compliance or obedience
in a manner not dissimilar to a protection racket (Spike Peterson 1992:
50--52). As Mark Neocleous notes, [[such dynamics]{.mark} serve to
**[neutralise radical]{.mark} political [action]{.mark}**]{.underline},
'encouraging us to surrender ourselves to the state in a thoroughly
conservative fashion' (2008: 4).

To understand how the pursuit of security intertwines with political
authority, it is important to recognise the dependent relationship
between security and insecurity. [Institutions and technologies of
[security can only]{.mark} [function in a context of
insecurities]{.mark}]{.underline}, which they may identify and seek to
pacify, but which they also need (and for which, of course, they are
often responsible). In Michael Dillon's terms, 'it is only because it is
contoured by insecurity, and because in its turn it also insecures, that
security can secure' (1996: 127). The nature and content of security
depends on its particular relationship with insecurity, with its
exclusions and violences and particular (political) designations of
threat. This regulative binary of security/insecurity intersects with
others that have similar effects, such as order/chaos, inside/outside
and sovereignty/anarchy. All of them regulate politics in a manner which
cements the place of political authority. On the latter dichotomy,
Richard Ashley's comments are pertinent:

On the one hand, the sign of 'sovereignty' betokens a rational identity:
a homogeneous and continuous presence that is hierarchically ordered,
that has a unique centre of decision presiding over a coherent 'self',
and that is demarcated from, and in opposition to, an external domain of
difference and change that resists assimilation to its identical being.
On the other hand, the sign of 'anarchy' betokens this residual external
domain: an aleatory domain characterised by difference and
discontinuity, contingency and ambiguity, that can be known only for its
lack of the coherent truth and meaning expressed by a sovereign
presence. 'Anarchy' signifies a problematic domain yet to be brought
under the controlling influence of a sovereign centre ... whether it be
an individual actor, a group, a class, or a political community.

(1988: 230)

As he identifies the conservatising regulation at the heart of the
sovereignty/anarchy dichotomy, so would I suggest that a similar process
is at work in the logic of security, privileging that which is
rationally bounded, coherent and compliant, and necessitating the
pacification or pathologisation of that which is not.

[Political imaginaries rooted in binary concepts limit our ethical
landscape in a variety of ways]{.underline}. As V. Spike Peterson
argues:

\[a\]s long as we remain locked in dichotomies, [we cannot accurately
understand and are less likely to transform social
relations]{.underline}: not only do oppositional constructions distort
the contextual complexity of social reality, [they set limits on the
questions we ask and the alternatives we consider]{.underline}. True to
their "origin" (Athenian objectivist metaphysics), the dichotomies most
naturalized in Western world views (abstract-concrete, reason-emotion,
mind-body, culture-nature, public-private) are both medium and outcome
of objectification practices. Retaining them keeps us locked in to their
objectifying-reifying-lens on our world(s) and who we are.

(1992: 54)

In such a context, **[[rather than seeking to rehabilitate
security]{.underline}]{.mark}** ([[and remain within this]{.mark}
security/insecurity [dichotomy]{.mark}]{.underline}), [[it might be more
productive to]{.mark} **resist, displace or [deconstruct
it]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

This is not a simple prospect; [refusing the social fantasy of security
would]{.underline}, in Jenny Edkins' terms, involve 'facing, on a
day-to-day basis, questions many of us prefer to forget, if we can', and
'would involve a shift away from the notion of sovereign state and
sovereign individual ... would [entail the development of a new vision
of political community]{.underline}, one that was not based on the
coming together of discrete participles to produce closed systems'
(2003: 368--369). While [the violent politics of security
is]{.underline} enacted through social institutions, it is also (as the
discussion above shows) [embedded in categories of thought]{.underline}.
The binaries of security/insecurity, order/chaos, sovereignty/anarchy
and more impose a theoretical domination which conditions political
possibility in particular authoritarian ways. As such, the task of
resistance might be to break down such binaries. [This may take place
through]{.underline} mocking, subverting or outwardly refusing the
closures such binaries enact (Rossdale forthcoming-a; Rossdale
forthcoming-b), or through [embracing the proliferation of definitions
of security as a]{.underline}n aporetic [space in which 'to think and
create **new social, ethical and economic relationships** outside
the]{.underline} oppressive structures of [political and epistemological
order']{.underline} (Burke 2007: 30--31).

What I want to suggest here is that we can interpret many practices of
activism and resistance as engaging in precisely this kind of resistance
to security/insecurity; that is, not just as affirming 'more ethical'
securities (though they may also do this), but as mounting a challenge
to the conceptual and political order of security more generally. In a
sense, this is not surprising, so often is resistance framed as that
insecurity, chaos and anarchy which necessitates securing, ordering and
sovereign gestures. It is also an unstable series of interventions,
liable to recuperation within a set of security discourses which swiftly
reposition challenge as threat. Nonetheless, these
[[resistances]{.underline}]{.mark} hold [[open spaces for an]{.mark}
[ethical critique not only of **particular orders of security**]{.mark},
[but]{.mark} more generally [of **the ways**]{.mark} **in which
[security orders]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

### AT: Realism

#### Realism's [inaccurate]{.underline} and [not inevitable]{.underline} in the Arctic---it's an [exceptional]{.underline} region, and [constructivist]{.underline} explanations of state behavior are more accurate.

**Heggelund, 23**---Master's candidate in International Affairs, School
of Social Sciences, University of Iceland (Ragna Mathiesen, "Thawing
Arctic Realities: Analyzing Arctic Security through the Lens of Arctic
Exceptionalism and Great Power Politics,"
<https://skemman.is/handle/1946/45722>, AC=Arctic Council, dml)

The findings from the discussion and analysis includes how [Arctic
exceptionalism **challenges** traditional]{.underline} security
theories, such as [great power politics]{.underline}, [by highlighting
the **unique vulnerabilities** and **interconnectedness** of security
issues]{.underline} in the Arctic region. [While great power politics
often prioritize **national interests** and
**competition**]{.underline}, [Arctic **exceptionalism** emphasizes the
need for **responsible governance** and **international
cooperation**]{.underline} to address environmental, human, and cultural
security concerns. The implications are that [security in the Arctic is
**not solely about military power or resource competition** but also
about preserving a fragile environment and safeguarding the well-being
of Arctic communities]{.underline}. [This]{.underline} approach
[underscores the importance of a **more holistic** and **cooperative**
security framework in the Arctic]{.underline}, [which can serve as a
**model** for addressing **global security challenges** that transcend
national borders]{.underline}.

Furthermore, [the emergence of great power competition in the
Arctic]{.underline} challenges the idea of Arctic exceptionalism as a
distinct security concept to some extent. While Arctic exceptionalism
emphasizes cooperation and environmental protection, great power
competition introduces elements of rivalry and resource-driven conflict.
This challenge [underscores the **complexity** of security dynamics in
the Arctic]{.underline}, [where both exceptional vulnerabilities and
geopolitical interests coexist]{.underline}. **[However]{.underline}**,
[Arctic exceptionalism can still play a **vital role** in mitigating the
negative consequences of great power competition by promoting
**responsible governance** and highlighting the **exceptional nature**
of Arctic challenges]{.underline}. It serves as a reminder that **[even
amid competition]{.underline}**, [the Arctic's unique vulnerabilities
must be addressed **collectively**]{.underline}.

In addition, an analysis of [Arctic exceptionalism as a distinct
security concept contributes to a **more nuanced understanding** of
security dynamics in the Arctic region and the global system by
acknowledging the **multifaceted nature** of security
challenges]{.underline}. It recognizes that [security is **not solely
about military threats** but also about **environmental**]{.underline},
**[human]{.underline}**, [and **cultural** vulnerabilities]{.underline}.
[This **nuanced understanding** helps policymakers and scholars address
security in a **comprehensive** manner]{.underline}, considering the
interconnectedness of global ecosystems and the shared responsibility to
protect them. Moreover, [it offers insights into how responsible
governance]{.underline}, [sustainable practices]{.underline}, [and
international cooperation can **effectively address** security
challenges]{.underline}, [not only in the **Arctic** but also in **other
regions** facing similar environmental and geopolitical
complexities]{.underline}. Therefore, Arctic exceptionalism serves as a
valuable framework for redefining security paradigms in a rapidly
changing world.

Also, in the analysis, [constructivism emerges as a **vital theoretical
framework** for comprehending the intricacies of Arctic security
dynamics]{.underline}. [It illuminates the power of **social
values**]{.underline}, **[norms]{.underline}**, [and **shared
assumptions** in shaping the behaviors and perceptions of states within
the Arctic region]{.underline}. [The inter-subjective knowledge and
shared interpretations among Arctic states **significantly influence**
their responses to environmental challenges]{.underline}, [driving
**cooperative efforts** and encouraging the pursuit of **common
interests** in environmental stewardship]{.underline}, [as exemplified
by the **AC**'s successes]{.underline}. Conversely, the lens of
[constructivism unveils the role of **shared interpretations** in great
power competition in the Arctic]{.underline}, [where states like
the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited **[S]{.underline}**tates,
**[Russia]{.underline}**, [and **China** vie for untapped resources and
emerging economic opportunities]{.underline}. [Constructivism further
underscores the impact of shared knowledge and interpretations on
**global diplomacy**]{.underline}, as seen through the AC's
achievements, [and on **scientific cooperation**]{.underline},
contributing to global efforts to combat climate change. The evolving
identities and norms in the Arctic, influenced by practices and
interactions, highlight constructivism's capacity to elucidate the
changing landscape of Arctic security and cooperation. Ultimately,
[constructivism adds **depth** to the understanding of how shared
beliefs and identities **shape Arctic exceptionalism** and the responses
of states to the challenges posed by the melting Arctic]{.underline},
[offering **valuable insights** into the region's security dynamics and
implications for the broader global system]{.underline}.

### AT: Reps Don't Shape Reality

#### Yes they do. 

**Grassl, 22**---Master's Candidate, Development and International
Relations, Aalborg University (Theresa, "Disrupting 'Arctic
Exceptionalism'? A Qualitative Discourse Analysis of the Securitization
of Climate Change in the EU's Arctic Policy,"
<https://projekter.aau.dk/projekter/files/472820096/Theresa_Grassl_Master_Thesis_final.pdf>,
dml)

[The employment of the Arctic as a means to **advance**]{.underline} the
**[securitization]{.underline}** of climate change [on a **global
scale** points toward a **key aspect** in Arctic relations]{.underline}:
[The Arctic **is**]{.underline}, [what outsiders **make it to
be**]{.underline}. The Arctic as an area is comprised of parts of the
territory of eight different states, whose decision-makers reside
outside the Arctic. The concept of Arctic exceptionalism displays a
similar construction of the Arctic from the outside as the EU's climate
change discourse does and centers around the cooperative relationship
between the Arctic states in comparison with their relationship outside
the Arctic. Moreover, a key aspect of critiques of the concept of Arctic
exceptionalism is that it is a narrow view of inter-state security and
does not account for other kinds of threats that affect local people
directly. Instead, as already mentioned when conceptualizing Arctic
exceptionalism, it is a selective discourse about characteristics of the
Arctic that enable cooperation on the international level.

The EU employing an exceptionalisation of the Arctic as part of its
efforts for the securitization of climate change differs from the
concept of Arctic exceptionalism. Hence, [the construction of the Arctic
by outsiders depends on their **understanding** of the
Arctic]{.underline}, [and]{.underline}, more importantly, [on whether a
certain depiction is **beneficial**]{.underline}. [For **Arctic
states**]{.underline}, [the **compartmentalization** of Arctic politics
and the depiction of the Arctic as **peaceful** is **essential** to
counter societal and environmental challenges]{.underline} and pursue
economic opportunities. [For the **EU**]{.underline}, [the depiction of
the Arctic as **severely affected** by climate change is
beneficial]{.underline}, as it serves to advance its aim for action
against climate change on a global scale.

[This shows how **differences** in the way that the Arctic is
**constructed** **affect inter-state relations** in the
Arctic]{.underline}. [There are **direct discursive
effects**]{.underline}: firstly, in the case of the concept of Arctic
exceptionalism, the upkeep of desecuritized relations, secondly, in the
case of the EU's climate change discourse, the reinforcement of climate
change as a threat. Additionally, it serves to increase insecurity in
interstate relations. For the Arctic states, this takes place [on two
levels]{.underline}. [On the one hand]{.underline}, [through the
**construction** of a **potential threat**]{.underline}, [which is
**contingent** on them **accepting** the securitizing move]{.underline}.
[On the other hand]{.underline}, [the securitizing move is also aimed at
a **global audience**]{.underline} in the global sphere, where the
Arctic states have different amounts of influence. [Even if the
securitizing move is **rejected** on a global level]{.underline}, [the
transfer of the Arctic to the global level **increases
insecurity**]{.underline}.

### AT: Threats Real

#### Not responsive---we never said they weren't!

**Greaves, 16**---Ph.D. candidate, Department of Political Science,
University of Toronto (Wilfrid, "Constructing In/Security in the Arctic:
Polar Politics, Indigenous Peoples, and Environmental Change in Canada
and Norway,"
<https://utoronto.scholaris.ca/items/e0790f25-319a-401b-a8c2-3149989fbc22>,
dml)

In its aims, CS [securitization theory is **explicitly not concerned**
with identifying **real** or **objective** security
threats]{.underline}, [but rather the **discursive process** through
which security threats are established]{.underline}.188 Even conceding
the role of facilitating conditions, the CS remains "radically
constructivist"189 in that in/security is determined by the success of
particular securitizing moves in elevating an issue to the fore of the
audience's consciousness and the apex of political priority. [Whether or
not a material danger corresponding to a securitizing move exists is
**irrelevant** to the **construction** of security threats]{.underline},
[just as the normative or practical implications of **responding** to a
particular threat are **inconsequential** to its **validity** as a
successful securitization]{.underline}. To the CS,
"'**[security]{.underline}**' [is]{.underline} thus a
**[self-referential]{.underline}** practice, [because it is in this
practice that the issue becomes a security issue]{.underline} --
[**not** necessarily because a **real existential threat** exists but
because the issue is **presented** as such a threat]{.underline}."190
[Successful securitizations may represent **objectively dangerous**
material phenomena]{.underline}, [or have **little** or **no basis** in
materiality]{.underline}, with the latter emerging either from a
subjective perception of threat or from instrumental use of security
language.

The CS view of [security]{.underline} as self-referential means it [is
neither characterized by the **absence** of a threat]{.underline}, per
se, [nor understood as a **binary opposite** to insecurity]{.underline}.
Instead, "[insecurity is the situation when there is a threat and no
defence against it]{.underline}; [security is a situation with a threat
and a defence against it]{.underline}."191 [Security is **not**
constituted by the **absence** of threats]{.underline}, [but by the
**specification** of a **threat** and a suitable
**defence-response**]{.underline}. By contrast, the absence of threats
-- not their material absence, but lack of issues socially constructed
as such -- is a condition outside the realm of security the CS refers to
as "normal politics."192 As Wæver explains:

[When there is no security problem]{.underline}, [we do not
conceptualize our situation in terms of security]{.underline}; instead,
[security is simply]{.underline} an **[irrelevant]{.underline}**
concern. [The statement]{.underline}, then, [that]{.underline} security
is always relative, and [one never lives in **complete
security**]{.underline}, [has the additional meaning that]{.underline},
[if one has such complete security]{.underline}, [one **does not label**
it]{.underline} '**[security]{.underline}**.' It therefore never
appears. Consequently, [transcending a security problem by politicizing
it **cannot happen** through thematization in security
terms]{.underline}, [only **away** from such terms]{.underline}.193

The Copenhagen School envisions all possible social issues falling along
a spectrum ranging from depoliticized-politicized-securitized. Issues
can move from politicized to securitized, but also from security back
into the realm of normal politics through the reverse process of
desecuritization. Throughout their work, the CS is clear in their
preference for issues to be desecuritized rather than the opposite. For
them, "[security should be seen as **negative**]{.underline}, [as a
**failure** to deal with issues as normal politics]{.underline}.
Ideally, [politics should be able to unfold according to routine
procedures **without** this extraordinary elevation of
specific]{.underline} '**[threats]{.underline}**' [to a prepolitical
immediacy]{.underline}."194 While the CS acknowledges that
securitization may sometimes be necessary, they always consider it
regrettable because of the societal cost to the normal operation of
political institutions, protection of particular rights, and the rule of
law.

This preference for desecuritization is one of the central distinctions
between the Copenhagen School and other theoretical approaches to
security, including subsequent accounts of securitization. Both
[objectivist]{.underline} and critical security [theories tend to
emphasize **maximizing** the provision of security]{.underline}, whether
through the expansion of material power for sovereign states or the
pursuit of "positive security" for individuals and communities.195 Wæver
dismisses this position, arguing "[the trick was and is to move from a
**positive** to a **negative** meaning]{.underline} ... **[We want less
security!]{.underline}**"196 For the CS, rather than more security for
threatened referent objects, "[desecuritization itself]{.underline} --
[as the **absence** of a world framed in terms of security]{.underline}
-- [is the]{.underline} emancipatory **[ideal]{.underline}**."197 [Their
position exhibits an unqualified preference for the operation of normal
politics **lacking** the **emergency connotations**]{.underline} and
anti-democratic potential [of securitization]{.underline}. It also
suggests the radicalism of the CS position, especially in the early
post- Cold War context in which the theory was first articulated:
arguing for less security was, and remains, a bold and counter-intuitive
approach to ISS.

### AT: Ukraine Makes Competition/Militarization/Securitization Inevitable

#### This is [uniqueness]{.underline} for us. Ukraine conflict doesn't make desecuritized Arctic policy [impossible]{.underline}, but [does]{.underline} make it [more necessary]{.underline}.

**Heggelund, 23**---Master's candidate in International Affairs, School

Arctic Realities: Analyzing Arctic Security through the Lens of Arctic

<https://skemman.is/handle/1946/45722>, dml)

[The **Russia-Ukraine** conflict has further **strained** Arctic
cooperation and governance]{.underline}. [The conflict has led to the
**suspension** of Arctic cooperation platforms and the **interruption**
of projects]{.underline} within the AC (Schreiber, 2022). The resulting
pause in communication and collaboration has implications for addressing
regional issues, sustainable development, and the well-being of Arctic
communities.

Moreover, the Russia-Ukraine war has revealed vulnerabilities within the
Arctic region, particularly in the context of climate change and the

**further burdened** the already vulnerable Arctic system and its

It has also highlighted Europe's dependence on Russian energy supplies

### AT: Utilitarianism

#### Utilitarian calculations are [unethical]{.underline} and justify [existentially genocidal violence]{.underline}.

**Oliver, 23**---Department of Philosophy, Vanderbilt University (Kelly,
"The Limits and Dangers of Risk-Benefit Analysis: From the Refugee
Crisis to the Coronavirus Pandemic," *The Vulnerability of the Human
World: Well-being, Health, Technology and the Environment*, Chapter 2,
pp. 13--27, SpringerLink, dml)

In Carceral Humanitarianism (2017), I argue against
[[util]{.mark}itarian risk-benefit [calculations]{.mark}]{.underline} as
a principle of ethics. There, I argue that the utilitarian calculus used
by both humanitarian aid organizations and so-called humanitarian
military operations (with its surgical strikes and statistical
modelling) [**reduces** human life to **statistical calculations** of
risk or benefit]{.underline}---and **[not]{.underline}** just risk or
benefit [in the **absolute**]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} rather risk
or benefit as defined by the organization or government doing the
calculations. Risks and benefits are [**always** defined with certain
goals in mind]{.underline}. [They [are **never**]{.mark} without
context]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} therefore [purely]{.underline}
"**[[objective]{.underline}]{.mark}**" [or **free** from]{.underline}
politics or **[bias]{.underline}**. Thus, any utilitarian calculus
assumes some good or goal beyond calculation such as pain or pleasure,
suffering or quality of life. [Even **survival**]{.underline}---life and
death---[are valued relative to particular goals that **defy** the
calculations of risk-benefit analysis]{.underline}. In other words,
employing risk-benefit and utilitarian calculations, requires making
assumptions about end goals and values that defy those very
calculations. It is not just that [there are **always** factors beyond
our control and variables we **do not know**]{.underline}, as the deadly
coronavirus makes painfully clear, but also that risk-benefit and
[[util]{.mark}itarian calculations [do not operate in a **vacuum**
but]{.mark} **[necessarily]{.mark}** make [assum]{.mark}ptions about
[the good]{.mark}]{.underline}.

In the West, risk-benefit analysis or [[util]{.mark}itarian calculative
reasoning]{.underline} has become the dominant mode of addressing social
issues, economic issues, and health issues. For example, Doctors without
Borders uses complex risk-benefit computer modeling to set policies
about where to send medical aid (Neuman, 2016).Footnote 9 And too many
hospitals dealing with the Covid-19 pandemic have had to resort to
triage medicine that uses risk-benefit analysis to distribute scarce
resources such as ICU beds, oxygen, and ventilators.

This type of calculative reasoning, however, [[treats]{.mark} human
[life as **fungible** and **exchangeable**]{.mark}]{.underline},
[and]{.underline} thereby [makes acceptable]{.underline} so-called
[**collateral damage** and **death**]{.underline}. The way risk-benefit
analysis is used to calculate acceptable collateral damage in warfare is
a prime example of the costs of calculable reason, where utilitarian
arguments are used to justify acceptable levels of killing and death.
[The calculating machine at the heart of utilitarian risk-benefit
analysis is **dangerous** when it makes individual lives fungible and
exchangeable]{.underline}. [[This]{.underline}]{.mark} approach [**does
not** and [**cannot** address the **singularity** of each living
being]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Both humanitarian aid and "humanitarian" warfare operate according to
logics of calculation. In fact, humanitarian warfare and humanitarian
aid operate in tandem. In a sense, nation states create refugees by
limiting citizenship, and then pay humanitarian aid organizations to
take care of them. National sovereignty and its concomitant notion of
citizenship are dependent upon so-called neutral humanitarian space,
which occupies an ambiguous space on the outskirts, and borders of, host
countries. Refugees are detained in camps and detention centers at the
borders in supposedly neutral humanitarian space free from politics and
war. Yet, this neutral humanitarian space, often guarded by state police
and military, is far from independent of state sovereignty.

To the contrary, humanitarian space is coopted to maintain national
sovereignty against the so-called threat of migrants, asylum seekers,
and refugees. In other words, within carceral humanitarianism, refugees
operate as both a threat to national sovereignty, and the justification
for shoring up borders.

The fact that the United States and the European Union pay other
countries to keep refugees from crossing into their borders makes clear
that the lives of these people can be bought and sold like so much cargo
to be held in port or lost at sea. For example, the recent crisis in
Belarus at the Polish border, where, according to migrants and refugees
are being used as political pawns. According to the New York Times:
"Europe has long paid other nations to keep refugees away from its
borders... This gives countries on the European periphery the leverage,
and incentive, to use those refugees as pawns" (see Fisher,
2021).Footnote 10

Policies that treat refugees as collateral damage or units of exchange,
where some lives are more valuable than others as calculated through
risk-benefit analysis, renders the lives of refugees fungible, and
therefore, ultimately, disposable.

Statistical models that compute [comparative valuations set out a
**hierarchy** of life]{.underline}, human and otherwise, [through which
violence is **justified**]{.underline}, [and some lives are
**valuable**]{.underline} (in this case, citizens), [while others are
**disposable**]{.underline} (in this case, refugees). Yet, [in **any**
risk-benefit or utilitarian calculation]{.underline}, [[there is
**always** a remainder that **cannot be
calculated**]{.underline}]{.mark}---not just in the arithmetic sense,
but also [a remainder beyond calculation and beyond
recognition]{.underline}.

[There is always [something we **don't know** that is]{.mark}
**necessarily [left out]{.mark}** of any calculation]{.underline}. [We
are **not masters** of the world and therefore there are always factors
**beyond** our knowledge and control]{.underline}. In addition, there
are elements beyond our comprehension. [It is **not**]{.underline},
then, [just a matter of **gathering all the relevant
data**]{.underline}, and that fact that we always fall short. [There are
elements that affect our calculations that we **aren't even aware
of**]{.underline}, [that we **can never know**]{.underline}. [There will
always be something **outside our comprehension affecting our
calculations**]{.underline}. Furthermore, [human
experience]{.underline}, and the experience of life itself, [**cannot be
reduced** to calculations or exchangeable units]{.underline}. [There is
always something about life that **transcends** even the most
sophisticated mathematical calculations]{.underline}. We might call this
the remainder left over when we attempt to translate ethics into
politics or the singular into the universal. There is always something
about the singularity of each living being that cannot be reduced to any
system of exchange value.

2.3 Justice Between Ethics and Politics

Justice requires continually renegotiating the relationship between
ethics and politics, singular and universal, precisely because there is
always something in excess, something beyond recognition.
[[This]{.underline}]{.mark} remainder [[is the **ethical imperative**
that]{.mark} should **keep us awake at night**]{.underline}. [[Rather
than]{.mark} lead to **inquietude** or **[apathy]{.mark}**]{.underline},
the excess beyond our [recognition [keeps us]{.mark} **actively
[searching]{.mark}** [for]{.mark} better ways to approach
[justice]{.mark} **[without]{.mark}** resting on]{.underline} moral
rules or [[risk-benefit calculations]{.underline}]{.mark} or
self-righteous principles. [The remainder]{.underline} beyond
calculation, beyond recognition, [[demands that we]{.mark} continue to
**[question]{.mark}** our]{.underline} own principles and
**[[policies]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [that we continue to examine our
concepts of]{.underline} justice and **[fairness]{.underline}** and
equality.

An [ethical politics]{.underline} is one in which justice demands the
hyper-vigilance self-critical reflection on where any response---ethical
or political---necessarily falls short. It is an ongoing task that
[**cannot end** with risk-benefit analysis]{.underline}. [Despite the
**ethical insomnia** and **political vertigo** caused by this
interminable self-questioning]{.underline}, [we must
**act**]{.underline}---[even **leap**]{.underline}. Although ultimately
the gap between singularity and universality is impassible, [[the
impossibility of]{.mark} achieving [justice is **no excuse** for not
**attempting it every single day**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

This tension between politics (which addresses itself to what can be
seen, and to the universal, what can be shared), and ethics (which
addressed itself to what cannot be seen, and to the singular, what
cannot be shared) is the knot at the center of justice.

[[Justice is **antithetical** to util]{.mark}itarian ethics insofar [as
individuals are **not fungible**]{.mark} **units** that can be
exchanged]{.underline}. [Utilitarianism relies on the **calculability**
of]{.underline} pain, suffering, **[survival]{.underline}**, [or the
quality of life]{.underline} itself. And the [utilitarian calculus
relies on determining **thresholds** that separate individuals into
groups]{.underline}, whether it is those who are members of the moral
community or the polis, and those who are not (for example animals), or
those [whose lives are **worth saving** and those whose lives are
**not**]{.underline}.

At some point, however, [this type of line-drawing **inevitably**
becomes either **political** or **arbitrary**]{.underline}. Take debates
over the basis for membership in the moral community. What are the
criteria for membership? Intelligence, language, reason, sentience, the
ability to suffer? And [whatever we decide should be the
criteria]{.underline}, [where do we **draw the line**?]{.underline} For
example, what counts as a language? Does the bees signal dance count?
Or, as some argue, is it merely a code and not a language? What counts
as intelligence? Peter Singer argues that great apes should be extended
rights or admitted to the moral community based on their intelligence,
which is greater than severely mentally disabled persons (see Singer,
1999; McMahan, 2009; Anstötz, 1993).Footnote 11 Some disability scholars
such as Eva Kittay have argued against this comparison, insisting that
human beings should be accorded special status regardless of their
mental abilities (Kittay, 1998a, b, 2009).Footnote 12 The controversy
over Singer's argument demonstrates how [utilitarian line-drawing is
always a **fraught endeavor** that ultimately rests on an **arbitrary
cut off point**]{.underline}.

For example, in Animal Liberation, Singer draws the line between calms
and shellfish, who supposedly cannot feel pain (Singer, 1990,
173--4).Footnote 13 But what if we learn that shellfish can feel pain?
In all these utilitarian endeavors, the liminal cases are unsettling
because they reveal the arbitrariness of borders and the inadequacy of
general classifications. This type of line-drawing or threshold not only
ignores the singularity of each individual, but also poses an arbitrary
or political dividing line as a natural border. The same is true of
national borders used to keep migrants out.

We see something similar in covid triage medicine where triage protocols
disadvantage whole classes of people. Old people, disabled people,
cancer survivors. The coronavirus lays bare the fantasy of calculable
criteria as an ethical way of life. Covid reveals gaping inequities in
the length and quality of life caused by social and economic
"pre-existing conditions." Celebrities and politicians from Madonna to
Andrew Cuomo called the coronavirus the "great equalizer." In March
2020, Cuomo tweeted "This virus is the great equalizer." In reality,
covid is the great unequalizer... Footnote 14

The pandemic forces doctors and nurses to make impossible decisions
about who is given the chance to live and who will die. But these
decisions exist in the context of pre-existing conditions of poverty,
racism, and unequal access to health care that skew any attempt at fair
or reasonable calculation. In a fair and just world, there would be
adequate resources available to everyone. Tragically, the real world is
not so fair or just. And [risk-benefit analysis and calculative
reasoning become the justification for **allowing some to
die**]{.underline}. [[When]{.mark} those [calculations]{.mark} pose as
**justice** or **fairness**]{.underline}, [and when they [allow us to
feel **certain**]{.mark}]{.underline}, even righteous, [[in our
**decisions**]{.underline}]{.mark}, then [[they risk a **slippery
slope**]{.mark} that leads us to a place [where **any** and **all life**
can become **disposable**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

The pandemic has made painfully clear the limitations of risk-benefit
analysis and calculative reasoning. Doctors and nurses report being
traumatized when forced to decide who will get life saving resources.
Face-to-face with patients, risk-benefit calculations are not as
straight forward as the mathematical or statistical modelling suggests.

A group of research physicians at the University of California,
describes the problem:

[Utilitarian approaches]{.underline} to ethical problems [often
**appear** to be **more practical** than many alternatives]{.underline}:
[we can **know** what the right course of action is by calculating
whichever choice will produce the **greatest good** for the **greatest
number**]{.underline}. Part of the appeal of utilitarian ethics is that
it seems like it should "work." [In the **real world**]{.underline},
however, [performing the relevant calculations can be **far from
easy**]{.underline}. This is especially true in crisis situations. [[It
may be **impossible** to collect]{.mark} the **[data]{.mark}**
needed]{.underline} as input for the calculations, it may be difficult
or impossible [to create the **decision rules**]{.underline} (predicting
prognosis or comparing outcomes) that constitute the calculations, [[the
calculator may be **impaired**]{.mark} by any number of different
factors]{.underline} (moral distress, fatigue, internal and external
pressures, etc.) [or **non-utilitarian moral constraints**]{.underline}
(such as superseding egalitarian policies, or even exponentially
increasing crisis severity) [can make utilitarian approaches
**infeasible**]{.underline}. (Fedyk et al., 2020)Footnote 15

2.4 The "Lessor of Evils"

[Rather than an ethical solution]{.underline}, [utilitarian risk-benefit
analysis]{.underline} in a triage medicine [is a]{.underline}
"**[lesser]{.underline}** of the **[evils]{.underline}**"
[approach]{.underline}. In fact, as I discovered doing research for
Carceral Humanitarianism, when discussing both humanitarian aid and
warfare, experts described risk-benefit and collateral damage
calculations as the "lessor of the evils" (Oliver, 2017). Some even see
these policies of assessing collateral damage as preventing the worst
evil by preventing massacre, genocide, and mass death. But as Hannah
Arendt reminds us, "[Those who choose the lesser evil **forget very
quickly** that they **still chose evil**]{.underline}" (Arendt, 2003,
30).Footnote 16

In Carceral Humanitarianism, I consider the question of "the worst"
violence. There, I argue that contemporary logics of proportionality
employed by both humanitarian aid organization and humanitarian military
operations are designed to mitigate and control violence and death to
avoid the worst violence and death, according to calculations of the
"lessor of evils." Thus, [we must ask what is [the]{.mark}]{.underline}
"most evil," or the [[**worst** evil]{.underline}.]{.mark} We may think
of genocide as the worst evil, and, in the West, "the worst" is often
shorthand for the "final solution" and the Holocaust. Since the end of
World War II, and the Cold War, "[the **worst**]{.underline}" [[has
become associated with]{.underline}]{.mark} the possibility of nuclear
war, even [the [destruction of the]{.mark} **entire
[planet]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

[[What if the worst is]{.mark} a result of [the **logic of calculation**
taken to the **extreme**]{.mark} at the expense of **all other
worldviews**?]{.underline} [What if the worst is [when this]{.mark}
calculable [way of thinking is used to justify
**massacre**]{.mark}]{.underline}[, **[genocide]{.underline}**, [and
**mass death**?]{.underline}]{.mark} [When lives become
**fungible**]{.underline} and exchange, then [we risk]{.underline} "[the
**worst**]{.underline}." Insofar as contemporary so-called
"humanitarian" warfare operates according to the logic of utilitarian
calculations of collateral damage that make some lives disposable, it
too risks "the worst."

[[The worst goes **beyond literal killing** and signals]{.mark} the
**[erasure]{.mark}**]{.underline} a way [[of]{.underline}]{.mark} seeing
the world---or we might as well say [[a **world
itself**]{.underline}]{.mark}---[by **covering it over** with another
worldview]{.underline}. In this regard, [comparative [models that
**reduce** life to]{.mark} units to be **exchanged** or **plugged into
[equations]{.mark}** [operate according to a **world-destroying
genocidal logic**]{.mark}]{.underline}. This is to say, when "the worst"
becomes part of the economy of "lessor of evils," life and death are
reduced to a logic of calculation that makes them fungible; one life is
weighed against another. Which is the lesser evil: One thousand four
hundred and twenty-two refugees who died in the Mediterranean Sea in
2020 or the cost to citizens of the European Union of providing safe
passage to refugees? Which is the lesser evil: killing one civilian
infant or killing three soldiers in a surgical strike? Which is the
lessor evil: a 50-year-old diabetic and mother of two with covid dying
because she doesn't have access to a ventilator, or a widowed and
otherwise healthy 70-year-old man dying without access to a ventilator?
Etc. Etc.

What is an acceptable death? The death as collateral damage? [To be
**calculable**]{.underline}, [each life must be **reducible** to a
common denominator and **exchangeable**]{.underline}. [Each life becomes
a unit in an equation]{.underline}, a risk factor.

Yet, as the examples I just mentioned suggest, [these [decisions as
to]{.mark} which is **worse**]{.underline}---[or which is [the
**lesser**]{.mark} of the [evils]{.mark}]{.underline}---[are
**[always]{.mark}** made by **[privileg]{.mark}ing [one
context]{.mark}** over another]{.underline}. Citizenship over foreign
nations. Civilians over military. Diabetes over motherhood. [[How do we
**weigh** one life against another?]{.underline}]{.mark} [How do we
decide whose life is worth **more**?]{.underline} Whose life is worth
more and whose life is expendable as collateral damage? [[Which factors
are]{.mark} deemed **[relevant]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark}
which are **[not]{.mark}**?]{.underline} Which goals are deemed worthy,
and which are not? Ultimately, [[util]{.mark}itarian calculus **[cannot
give us the answers]{.mark}** to these questions]{.underline}.

### AT: "You-Link-You-Lose"=Bad

#### "You-link-you-lose" is a [circus caricature]{.underline} of our framework that [proves]{.underline} its necessity.

**Jacobsen, et al, 24**---Assistant Professor at the Royal Danish
Defence College's Centre for Arctic Security Studies (Marc, with Ole
Wæver and Ulrik Pram Gad, "Introduction: Analyzing Greenland in Arctic
Security," *Greenland in Arctic Security: (De)securitization Dynamics
under Climatic Thaw and Geopolitical Freeze*, Introduction, pp. 18-19,
ST=security theory, acronym expansion denoted by brackets, dml)

More important for our purpose, however, is that [the point of doing
analysis informed by a theory is **not** just to be able to **check a
box** by deciding that]{.underline} '**[yes]{.underline}**, [this
instance **lives up** to the criteria]{.underline} specified in the
theory, [so I **hereby declare it security**]{.underline}.' Rather, [the
point is to learn from **when** and **how** the empirical world **does
not quite match** the theory]{.underline}. In this view, "[a theory
is]{.underline} basically [a **model** that can be **held against
empirical instances** to assess structural similarity]{.underline}"
(Wæver 2011). [It is]{.underline}, of course, [**important** to know if
the]{.underline} melting of **[Arctic]{.underline}** ice [is
**securitized**]{.underline}. [It is **even more important** to know
**how** it is securitized]{.underline}, [both because it may inform our
**politics**]{.underline} in relation to climate change [and because it
may inform our **conception** of how who may be able to securitize
what]{.underline}. But [it is also important to know about **partially
successful** securitizations]{.underline}, **[surprising
misfires]{.underline}** (Åtland and Ven Bruusgaard 2009),
[and]{.underline} '**[weird]{.underline}**' [dynamics that resemble
those described as]{.underline} '**[standard]{.underline}**' by the
theory. When it comes to theory, [the **proof of the pudding** is not
just in the **eating**]{.underline}, [the proof]{.underline} of the
pudding [is also in the **making**]{.underline}. When asking ourselves
whether it makes sense taking ST on tour from its late 20thcentury
European point of departure, [the sense to be made comes not just from
deciding whether They do security as We do]{.underline}, [but also by
learning about how dynamics that resemble the core propositions of the
theory **come out differently** under circumstances]{.underline} further
and further removed from the theory's ideal type (in terms of sector,
geography, culture, age, etc.). Does ST tell us something about the
dynamics at hand, something new and unexpected that makes it possible
for actors to reorient their action? Or does ST's failure to capture a
case tell us something interesting about the core of the theory or about
the dynamics analyzed? Is it not the case [with a lot of
theories]{.underline} in both natural and social sciences that [we learn
by **applying** them and then **observing anomalies** that **could not
have been found** or **understood** had the theory just been **deemed
irrelevant**]{.underline}; [it is exactly the]{.underline}
'**[model]{.underline}**' [that allows one to see **what does not fit**
it]{.underline}. [This is why taking]{.underline} ST
\[[security]{.underline} theory\] [out of its]{.underline} '**[comfort
zone]{.underline}**' in European post--Cold War security [is
important]{.underline}. ST's ongoing world tour is important in telling
us both dynamics 'out there' that looks more or less like security, but
also in telling us about how Europe is more or less provincial and/or
how the world may or may not be in a process that will make ST obsolete.

## Aff

### Framework---2AC---Core

#### Only vote for them if they [rejoin the plan]{.underline} by proving there's a [unique opportunity cost]{.underline}, otherwise the alt's not [mutually exclusive]{.underline} with the plan. Two impacts:
