### AT: Liberalism Bad

#### Liberal norms of law and sovereignty [reduce international conflict]{.underline}.

**Gill-Tiney, 22**---Lecturer, Department of Politics and International
Relations, University of Oxford (Patrick, "A Liberal Peace?: The Growth
of Liberal Norms and the Decline of Interstate Violence," Journal of
Conflict Resolution, 2022, Vol. 66(3), 413--442, dml)

This article shows that [[liberal]{.mark} interpretations of
**[sovereignty]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [which [emphasize]{.mark}
**[i]{.mark}nternational [law]{.mark}**]{.underline},
**[[interdependence]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[free
[trade]{.mark}]{.underline}**, [**[democracy]{.mark}** [and]{.mark}
**individual [rights]{.mark}** and **freedoms**]{.underline}, [have
become **increasingly prevalent**]{.underline} in UN Security Council
resolutions since 1970. Given that two non-western states, Russia/Soviet
Union and China, are permanent members, I argue that the content of
these resolutions reflects broad consensus between major powers---both
western and non-western---as how these norms should be interpreted. This
is not the same as arguing that these states share preferences or
interests, rather, substantial differences remain, largely along the
cleavage between the United States, Britain and France on the one hand
and China and Russia on the other (Einsiedel and Malone 2018, 156-58).1
Yet, the collective positions of these states have evolved over time,
suggesting a shift in how sovereignty is understood. The dominant role
that these states have in shaping the international order means that
[their collective understandings may be taken as representative of the
**normative structure** of international society at any point in
time]{.underline}, [with the expectation that this impacts **all**
states in the system]{.underline}.2

I argue that [[as liberal]{.mark} interpretations of these fundamental
[norms **increase**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[the **likelihood
of**]{.underline}]{.mark} a dispute participant resorting to
**[[violence decreases]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Through content analysis
of all UN Security Council resolutions between 1970 and 2014 I first
create a measure of the strength of liberal interpretations of
sovereignty. This is then utilized in quantitative analysis of dispute
participants in the period to explain the variation in the level of
violence employed. [I find **statistical** and **substantive support**
for my theory]{.underline}, [showing **[empirically]{.mark}** that [the
growth]{.mark} in liberal norm interpretations [is **negatively
associated**]{.mark} with the likelihood of]{.underline} a state
resorting to [violence]{.underline} in an interstate dispute.

[This is [not]{.mark} to argue [that structural]{.mark}
[arg]{.mark}ument[s]{.mark} [are **wrong**]{.mark}]{.underline}, far
from it, [nor is it to **reject** the roles of conventional and nuclear
capabilities]{.underline} in shaping interstate conflict behavior.
[[Rather]{.underline}]{.mark}, **[[state power does
matter]{.underline}]{.mark}**, that is why the collective
interpretations of the permanent members of the UN Security Council are
used to understand the system. Domestic veto players, intergovernmental
enforcement mechanisms, and trade dependency all do shape the responses
of policymakers during an interstate dispute. However, [the
**intersubjective ideas** they carry with them into disputes
are]{.underline} also [**crucial** to understand why some disputes
**escalate**]{.underline}, [and others do **not**]{.underline}.

This article contributes to our understanding of the role that ideas
have in shaping interstate conflict in two ways. Firstly, I contribute
to the large literature which has sought to understand the role which
norms play in selecting for certain behaviors over others. Most of the
work within this area has been qualitative and has either charted the
development of specific bundles of norms over long periods (Keene 2012,
2013; Risse, Ropp, and Sikkink 1999; Sandholtz 2007) or focused on the
life-cycle of specific norms (Eckstein 1988; Finnemore 2000; Finnemore
and Sikkink 1998; Krook and True 2012; Nadelmann 1990; Sandholtz 2007;
Sandholtz and Stiles 2008). I build upon these insights by arguing that
the norm of sovereignty has been re-interpreted over time, and that the
collective nature of reinterpretation means that it can be used to
examine its impact in interstate conflict.

Secondly, this article provides an innovative means of exploring ideas
in a quantitative framework. The quantitative interstate conflict
literature has heavily favored structural and power-based explanations
for actor behavior. This has come at the particular expense of norms.
Whilst there is widespread acceptance that norms matter amongst these
scholars, they have tended to set aside norm-based explanations given
the difficulty of objective measurement. This difficulty revolves around
the problem of having surety that multiple actors share an idea, that
is, intersubjective understanding. The most obvious sources of data to
assess this would be a survey of policymakers or the content of
international legal documents. This first avenue is incredibly difficult
to gain anything but anecdotal responses too. Policymakers are hard to
get access to and may misrepresent their own thoughts and actions to
show themselves in as best light as possible. Ensuring that any
respondents have understood the survey in the same way is difficult
given language differences, whilst response rates are likely to be so
low as to prevent a non-random sample from being acquired, even if the
potential pool of relevant participants is widened to include all senior
politicians and bureaucrats, both incumbent and preceding.

Some of these problems are resolved by utilizing the texts of
international legal documents. Given that these are generally carefully
and cautiously written the researcher is more certain that the drafters
actually share an understanding of the topic. Recent work by Allee and
co-authors (Allee, Elsig, and Lugg 2017; Allee and Lugg 2016) have
innovatively utilized content analysis of interstate trade deals to
explore how much is replicated in subsequent deals, which sheds light on
the relative power of the participants. This has certainly gained deeper
understanding of the topic, but is not a suitable approach for this
research questions for two reasons. Firstly, these deals are generally
drafted by small numbers of states, that is, they may not be assumed to
represent the preferences and interests of nonparties. Secondly, whilst
interpretations are spelled out, these may be mechanistic rather than
ideational, making it unclear what the broader normative position may
be. This is made more acute by the drafting being down by bureaucrats
and lawyers rather than policymakers themselves. Whilst some direction
is no-doubt given by the latter, assuming a high level is problematic.
Utilizing the content of UN Security Council resolutions addresses these
problems because the documents may be assumed to be political, that is,
though member states are represented by ambassadors, a high-level of
involvement by their state governments is typical given the importance
attached to security issues. Moreover, since the permanent membership is
diverse in interests and preferences, and these members can veto
unpalatable resolutions, they may be reasonably assumed to provide
insight into intersubjective understandings.

Existing Explanations of Interstate Conflict and the Role of Norms

Two existing literatures are built on here, firstly, the broad
democratic peace literature, including its offshoots and opponents, and
secondly, scholarship on the evolution of international norms. The
former literature initially focused upon regime type, and over time has
been widened to include more fine-grained analyses of domestic political
structures in any state (Bell and Quek 2018; Fearon 1994; Gries et al.
2020; Huth and Allee 2002; Tomz, Weeks, and Yarhi-Milo 2020; Weeks 2012;
Weiss 2013; Weiss and Dafoe 2019). However, the approach in all of these
works is to examine structures, that is, formal institutional
constraints encountered by decision makers, rather than norms, that is,
the role of shared principles, perceptions and expectations of behavior.
The distinction between norms and structure is not rigid. This is most
clearly seen in the term "institution" which is commonly used to mean
both formal structures which limit the agency of decision makers, but
also a set of norms, rules, and practices which govern a specific area.
To clarify the distinction being made in this article, I adapt the
nomenclature used by Reus-Smit (1999) who distinguishes between
constitutional institutions, norms without which a society of states
could not exist, and fundamental institutions, which facilitate
cooperation. The former includes sovereignty, as well as the rights of
self-determination and nonintervention, and the latter contractual
international law and the organizations which enforce it (both internal
and external to states). In this article, I term the former "norms" and
the latter "structures."

The democratic peace literature has spurred several offshoots, the most
relevant of which are those which have argued that rather than regime
type the "peace" is generated by economic factors including trade
interdependency and level of development (Gartzke 2007; Hegre 2000; Kim
2014; Kinne 2012; McDonald 2009, 2010; Mousseau 2013; Schneider and
Gleditsch 2010) or shared intergovernmental organization membership
(Appel 2018; Kinne 2013a, 2013b; Prorok and Appel 2014). More general
critiques have argued that any effect seen is due to the prior
settlement of territorial disputes (Gibler 2012; Gibler and Tir 2014;
Owsiak 2012), nuclear weapons (Waltz 1990), alliances (Kydd 2005),
unipolarity (Monteiro 2014) or military power (Layne 1994; Rosato 2003)
instead. With the exception of the latter critiques, this debate has
broadly accepted the importance of norms (Doyle 1986; Moravcsik 1997;
Owen 1994; Russett and Oneal 2001), especially as the "good" functioning
of domestic and international structures, particularly veto players,
requires political actors to have the right norms.

This relationship between norms and structures has been more closely
addressed through the focus on contracts, that is, to hone in on
specific economic norms and structures characterized as allowing an
economic market to function "with extensive and regularized transactions
among strangers that require an element of trust" (Mousseau and Xiongwei
2018, sec. Emergence Contractualist Peace). This literature argues that
the relationship between peace and democracy is spurious, and rather,
that both are explained by "contracturalist" norms and structures (Gelpi
and Grieco 2008; Mousseau, Hegre, and O'Neal 2003), which builds upon
the work of (Acemoglu and Robinson 2006; Dahl 1997; North, Wallis, and
Weingast 2009). Yet, the literature still relies upon
operationalizations which are essentially structural, that is, whilst
recognizing the importance of norms, the empirical testing has fallen
short.

A comparatively small subset of the literature has examined norms as an
explanation for the democratic peace. For Deutsch (1957), though NATO is
understood as a structure, a security community is more crucially formed
around shared values (norms). Kahl argues that the basis of the
democratic peace is collective identity rooted in liberal democracy
interlinked by intergovernmental organizations (1998). For Risse, the
influence of NATO allies upon the foreign policy of the United States
demonstrates the importance of liberal norms, through a mechanism which
relies upon the similarity of domestic political institutions in these
liberal democracies and coalition-building (1995). Though valuable
additions to the literature, and in Deutsch's case systematic in its
treatment of the subject, these works fail to tackle the question of
whether the democratic peace is structural or norm based since the two
are entwined throughout. The difficulty in conceptualizing, isolating
and operationalizing norms is a clear problem. Broadly, it is hard to
isolate a norm from either the structure in which it exists or from
other norms for analysis. It is also a challenge to show that any norm
is truly intersubjective in understanding or to measure its effect upon
some outcome of interest. Yet, without attempts to do so we are left
with a partial picture, particularly for those emphasizing the
importance of structures---be these domestic or international---for
reducing interstate conflict. In short, the existence of a domestic
legislature, independent judiciary, or interstate treaties, is only
relevant if the individuals which employ them do so appropriately. To
understand this necessitates exploring the ideas in the heads of these
individuals, and particularly, how these ideas are shared.

In contrast to the large, predominantly quantitative, literature which
has examined the democratic peace and wider questions linking regime
type to the onset, conduct and termination of interstate disputes and
wars, the broader literature on interstate norms has tended to be
qualitative and highly focused. This has resulted in detailed work on
human rights (Risse, Ropp, and Sikkink 1999), humanitarian intervention
(Wheeler 2000), and nuclear weapons (Rublee and Cohen 2018; Tannenwald
1999). There is no doubt that this has contributed to our understanding
of interstate relations, yet the clear shortcoming is generalizability.
That is, the specificity of these approaches, necessary to make any form
of causal claim, is in a sense also the undoing.

In this article I focus on the reinterpretation of the sovereignty norm
by powerful states. I argue that they have a dominant role in shaping
the normative landscape of the international system by being tasked with
the interpretation of norms and the maintenance of international society
(Hurrell 2007; Wight 1978; Saunders 2006). Operationalizing this, I
utilize UN Security Council resolutions as a means of assessing what the
consensus position of the permanent members is. Content analysis is used
to examine the normative framing of resolutions, allowing both an
exploration of change over time, and quantitative measures of the
relative strength of these ideas to be derived which can then be used in
regression analysis.

The Constraining Effect of Liberal Interpretations of Sovereignty

Norms are dynamic, and undergo processes of evolution, creation and
destruction. Change may occur gradually as actors shift their
understandings towards an alternative, or suddenly through systemic, and
thereby social, change. In international relations, the latter is
tightly linked to sudden shifts in great power authority, which has
typically occurred due to defeat and a subsequent realignment of
international order. War, and its aftermath, can lead to substantial
rearrangements of international order, meaning both a new distribution
of power, but also a re-interpretation of international society. In
examining change in understandings of sovereignty I term gradual change
"evolution" and sudden change "tectonic."

Interpretations of Sovereignty

The post-World War Two era allows examination of both evolutionary and
tectonic change in understandings of sovereignty. Evolution occurs
during relative peace, whilst tectonic change is part of major
realignments of international order. The end of the Cold War, though
non-violent certainly led to significant power shifts, resulting in a
tectonic change. The pre- and post-Cold War eras are eras of evolution.
Assessing rivalrous interpretation of norms is crucial to understanding
these changes. I outline a divergence between traditional and liberal
interpretations which are used to examine change in norms, and the
effect upon international conflict.

Over the post-World War Two period two interpretations of sovereignty
have existed, firstly, that emphasizing territorial sovereignty and
non-interference in the domestic affairs of others, and secondly, that
emphasizing international law, interdependence, free trade, democracy
and individual rights and freedoms in international affairs. I term the
former approach to sovereignty "traditional," and the latter "liberal."
A liberal interpretation of sovereignty, therefore, is one which places
emphasis upon the actions of states, particularly those between a
government and its citizens. Sovereignty in this understanding implies
not only the exercise of power over a territory and people (as in the
traditional understanding), but also that there are limits to the
exercise of power and duties to a people (Sandholtz and Stiles 2008,
287-88).3 This has been most clearly stated in the Responsibility to
Protect doctrine. I examine the relative balance between these two
interpretations over time, arguing that this balance alters how
sovereignty is understood, and thereby is related to the likelihood of
escalation in interstate disputes.

My expectation is that the relative balance between these two modes of
interpretation has shifted over the post-World War Two period to favor a
liberal interpretation. This does not mean that traditional
interpretations are irrelevant, rather, that they have been
de-emphasized in international discourse relative to the latter. A
spectrum in which purely traditional and purely liberal interpretations
mark the poles is conceived of as existing, with great powers occupying
positions between the two. The effect that this shift toward a liberal
interpretation of sovereignty has occurred on international conflict
escalation is now examined.

Understandings of Sovereignty and Conflict Escalation

How understandings of sovereignty impact international disputes may be
explored in terms of the conflict life-cycle, that is, why a dispute
emerges, why violent conflict breaks out, and how the conflict ends, or
in terms of participant conduct during war. Here, I examine the effect
of change in the collective understanding of sovereignty upon
escalation, meaning that I assume the prior existence of international
disputes. Whilst the effect of norms upon the onset of disputes is an
important question, very few disputes result in the use of violence,
that is, though onset is a necessary condition for violence, it is far
from sufficient. I examine escalation from a nonviolent dispute to a
violent dispute---the use of armed force in international
relations---because this is of greater substantive significance to state
leaders and their citizens. Moreover, whilst reducing the likelihood of
dispute onset would likely result in less international violence, [the
more normatively important question is how to avoid
**escalation**]{.underline}, [as this **reduces the costs** of
war]{.underline}: human death and economic destruction.

This is not to trivialize dispute onset, and logically we might expect
the growth of liberal understandings of sovereignty to also reduce onset
rates as well as escalation likelihood. However, there are two reasons
why we may doubt the substantive significance of this, firstly, dispute
onset is a relatively low threshold, and secondly, onset is often
triggered by events beyond the direct control of a state. To this first
point, a dispute simply means that two or more actors disagree over
something, oftentimes the behavior of the other side or the distribution
of some good (for instance, territory). This article utilizes
Militarized Interstate Disputes as the operationalization of dispute,
and many disputes are comparatively minor events, with the threshold
being that one of the two parties has a minimum level of militarization
as part of the dispute (for example, the issuance of a deterrent, or
compellent, threat). A focus on onset, therefore, would prevent
observation of substantively more important actions, that is, the actual
use of force against another. Secondly, many disputes begin due to
accident and/or the decision making of low-level military commanders or
political officials who are neither in the position to consider national
interests nor strategic interactions. This means that onset may
essentially be an apolitical decision which takes little or no account
of the broader strategic landscape nor the structural and normative
context. It is after onset, that is, when escalation is considered by
policymakers, that the stakes are higher and when causal mechanisms
common to this topic are most active.

[Escalation]{.underline} then, [is a **political**
decision]{.underline}. In making the choice to escalate, or not,
[policymakers are]{.underline} impacted by a range of strategic factors,
but crucially, are also [constrained by **norms**]{.underline}. [The
[use of force]{.mark} in international relations [must be
**justified**]{.mark}]{.underline}, and since 1945 the UN Charter
specifies just two: self-defense and collective peace enforcement. To
this, we might also add humanitarian intervention, though this remains
contested. As a baseline, therefore, when faced with dispute onset,
policymakers must justify their actions with reference to one of these
reasons. Moreover, [[in making the **decision to
escalate**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[policymakers are]{.mark} also
**[constrained by liberal values]{.mark}** which emphasize
diplomacy]{.underline}, [cooperation]{.underline}, [international
law]{.underline}, [international organizations]{.underline}, [the rights
of the individual]{.underline}, [and to question the legitimacy of the
march to war]{.underline}. Taken [together]{.underline}, [[this **slows
the decision to escalate**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[since legal
justification is **needed**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[diplomacy is
**necessary**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[and potential]{.mark} economic and
human costs **[understood]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [**calculated** and
**defended**]{.underline}. [A shift toward liberal interpretations
thereby means a **reduced probability of escalation** to
violence]{.underline}, tacitly assuming that the risk of a dispute
arising in the first-place is unchanged. This leads me to my first
hypothesis, which relates to the evolution mechanism of change:

Hypothesis 1: [Given an international dispute]{.underline}, [the risk of
escalation **decreases** as liberal interpretations
**increase**]{.underline}.

An alternative explanation is that any impact of normative change is
actually a result of the end of the Cold War, that is, the
redistribution of power between great powers, typically resulting from
system-wide conflict results in qualitative differences in
interpretations of fundamental norms between peace-periods. Whilst the
end of the Cold War did not involve a system-wide war, it did mark the
end of sustained rivalry between great powers. The pre- and post-
periods can thereby be compared. Given the rivalry of the United States
and Soviet Union during the Cold War, and the relative dominance of the
United States post-Cold War, we might expect qualitative differences in
conflict behavior to be observable in the two time periods,
attributable, perhaps, to a greater emphasis upon liberal
interpretations in the post-Cold War era. The dramatic change in the
distribution of power triggered a significant change in the consensus
surrounding interpretation of fundamental norms of international
society. This leads me to my second hypothesis, which captures the
tectonic change mechanism:

Hypothesis 2: [The risk of dispute escalation decreases given a systemic
increase in **liberal interpretations of sovereignty**]{.underline}
attributable to the end of the Cold War.

Method

I use multiple logistic regression to estimate the effect of change in
the normative landscape on the use of force by dispute participants. To
address the underlying selection problem, that is, that dispute onset is
non-random, I also estimate maximum likelihood and twostep Heckman
selection models. Substantive significance from the logistic regression
is then explored utilizing the observed values for predicted
probabilities approach (Hanmer and Ozan Kalkan 2013). Standard errors
are robust and clustered by year, where model specification allows (that
is, not in the Heckman twostep model). This is to take account of the
data structure since years differ by the number of Militarized
Interstate Disputes (MIDs) which onset and the number of participants in
these MIDs. Because the independent variables are annual values, the
same value is given to all dispute participants in the same year. If
standard errors were not clustered by year then there would be
substantial withincluster correlation.

[This]{.underline} paper [models]{.underline} the hostility level of
interstate [disputes as a **monadic** phenomenon]{.underline}. [Much of
the literature]{.underline} on democratic peace theory [utilizes a
**dyadic** setup]{.underline}, either directed or undirected. I opt for
a monadic structure for three reasons, firstly, because [many of the
theoretical explanations for the democratic peace are]{.underline} in
essence, **[monadic]{.underline}** explanations, with a dyadic version
layered on top. For instance, the core claim of much of the literature
is that democracy reduces the hostility of foreign policy, a monadic
claim, because leaders are accountable to citizens who have a low
tolerance for the costs or war, and because legislatures and a free and
independent media provides greater scrutiny of policymaking, slowing the
march to war. The dyadic version of this---the interaction of joint
democracy---relies on the signals that these same processes provide to
another actor. It is puzzling, therefore, that empirical findings have
tended to show support for the latter, but not for the former. Secondly,
[the **difficulty** in finding empirical support for any monadic
explanation means that [a **higher threshold** is]{.mark} being
[set]{.mark} here [by]{.mark} using [a monadic
approach]{.mark}]{.underline}. Thirdly, the argument I make, that
liberal norms reduces the hostility level of interstate disputes, may
have varying effects according to the regime type of dyads. However,
that claim is not made here, rather I argue that these norms exist for
all in international society, not for a subset, and that they act
directly upon policymakers, not only when policymakers interact with
their international counterparts.

### AT: Neorealism Wrong

#### Neorealism's [true]{.underline} in the Arctic.

**Stojkovic, 21**---M.A. Candidate, Governance and Public Policy,
Universität Passau (Dominik, "The Race for the Arctic: A Neorealist Case
Study of Russia and the United States,"
<https://www.e-ir.info/2021/05/17/the-race-for-the-arctic-a-neorealist-case-study-of-russia-and-the-united-states/>,
dml)

[Neorealism]{.underline}, as discussed, [predicts that states will
attempt to **maximize security** by using military power]{.underline}.
Therefore, they may pursue several strategies, including power
projection, increasing economic capabilities, and seeking alliances. To
pursue security in a self-help system, Moscow and Washington should
increase military and latent power to, ultimately, reach regional
hegemony. The brief case study shows that, [following the]{.underline}
largest [retreat of sea ice]{.underline} in 2007 ('the shock'), [Russia
began **increasing its Arctic activity** by claiming
resource-rich]{.underline} ([e.g.]{.underline}, **[Lomonosov
ridge]{.underline}**) [and strategically significant]{.underline}
([e.g.]{.underline}, [the **NSR**]{.underline}) [territory to boost its
national power]{.underline}. Besides, [it increased its partnership with
**China** and focused on **infrastructure development** to raise
economic performance]{.underline}.

As noted in Chapter I, Mearsheimer believes that all great powers strive
to accumulate power over rivals, with the ultimate goal of hegemony (cf.
Mearsheimer 2001, p. 40). While [the US]{.underline} is the most
powerful state in the system, it [**cannot dominate** the
**world**]{.underline}. Due to its minor role [in the
**Arctic**]{.underline}, [it motivates other states to increase their
power to secure their position in the system]{.underline}. With
Washington largely absent from the Arctic, Russia expanded its economic
investment and military activity, becoming the regional hegemon as
defined by Mearsheimer (2001, p. 232).

[Neorealism differentiates **latent** and **military**
power]{.underline} (cf. Keohane 1984, p. 55). [When calculated in
**both** terms]{.underline}, [the **balance of power** between Russia
and other Arctic states **drastically favors** Russia]{.underline}. With
the largest Arctic territory and population, [it generates a
**significant amount** of its latent power in this resource-rich
area]{.underline}. [This allows it to operate the largest fleet of
**icebreakers**]{.underline}, [Arctic **military bases**]{.underline},
[and **troops**]{.underline}. [In these **quantitative**
terms]{.underline}, [the power structure in the Arctic is
**clear**]{.underline}: [Russia is a **great power** in the High
North]{.underline}, [and the US]{.underline}, with only one functioning
icebreaker and not a single independent Arctic military base, [is
**not**]{.underline}.

[This has **substantial implications** for the Arctic
region]{.underline}. Mearsheimer states that [if the structure is
**multi-polar** and **unbalanced**]{.underline}, [it will be **much more
prone** to **conflict** since regional powers seek
dominance]{.underline}. Other states may then seek alliances (cf.
Mearsheimer 2001, p. 344). [This is where]{.underline} Walt's **[balance
of threat]{.underline}** approach [becomes a **useful analytical
tool**]{.underline}. In predicting the formation of alliances between
states, Walt (1987, pp. 21-22) defines threat perception as a crucial
determinant. The threat --in this structure-- comes from Moscow. [As a
**reaction** to Russian power projections and military
build-up]{.underline}, [neorealism would expect smaller powers to
**pursue security** by bandwagoning with Washington]{.underline}.

[This can be observed in **several collaborations**]{.underline}: [while
**Canada**]{.underline}, **[Denmark]{.underline}**, [and **Norway** have
collectively increased their Arctic military activity in partnership
with the US through **NATO**]{.underline}, [**Finland** and **Sweden**
are conducting regular Arctic military training with Norway and the
US]{.underline} (cf. Hultqvist 2020). Eventually, [**Denmark** and
**Iceland** grant US military presence on their territories]{.underline}
in Keflavik and Thule.

Finally, [although the Arctic is shaped by cross-national
**cooperation** through forums and regimes]{.underline} (e.g., UNCLOS,
AC), [we can find **evidence** that states **ignore** or **break** the
rules established by such institutions]{.underline}: while President
**[Putin]{.underline}** officially [claimed that]{.underline} "\[a\]ll
[decisions by the Arctic Council are nothing more than
**recommendations**]{.underline}" (Putin 2019), [**Canada** and the
**US** boycotted an AC meeting following Russia's intervention in
Ukraine]{.underline} (cf. Rahbek-Clemmensen 2017). Moreover, [**NATO**
issued a **general ban** on cooperation with Russia and]{.underline},
due to conflicting interests, [a total of **15 bilateral events**
between Oslo and Moscow were canceled]{.underline} (cf. ibid.).
[Although giving **lip service** to Arctic cooperation]{.underline},
[Russia increased **flights**]{.underline}, [violated NATO-states'
**border sovereignty**]{.underline}, [and conducted
unauthorized]{.underline}
**[i]{.underline}**nter**[c]{.underline}**ontinental
**[b]{.underline}**allistic **[m]{.underline}**issile [**tests** in the
region]{.underline} (cf. ibid.). Finally, due to the denial of climate
change by the former US government, the AC was unable to release an
official declaration after their meeting in 2019 (cf. Lanteigne 2019).

Conclusion

[Although neorealism has been]{.underline} much [criticized]{.underline}
for its revisionist character and inability to explain change (cf.
Korab-Karpowicz 2017, Ch. 3.2), [it has provided **appropriate
assumptions** in an opening Arctic]{.underline}. The study found that
[thawing ice caps led the Arctic states to **pursue resources** to boost
their economic and military power]{.underline}. Moreover, this pursuit
of economic development has increased human activity and security
threats in the region, [leading to an **expansion** in Arctic military
capability]{.underline} by both Russia since 2007 and, more recently,
the US. Furthermore, the paper investigated Arctic power structures and
found that Russia's regional geographic, economic, and demographic
domination resulted in weaker Arctic states' bandwagoning behavior. This
was primarily observable in military cooperation through NATO and the
reopening and development of US bases on Icelandic and Greenlandic
territories. Finally, [as neorealism would **expect**]{.underline}
states to ignore the rules established by international regimes when it
is in their interest to do so, the illegal penetration of NATO airspace,
the boycott of AC meetings, and the refusal to make joint declarations
provided some grounds.

### AT: Realism Bad

#### Realism enables [radical resistance]{.underline} to domination---at [worst]{.underline}, there's [no link]{.underline} to their Ks because realism isn't a [monolith]{.underline}.

**Franceschet, 24**---Professor, Department of Political Science,
University of Calgary (Antonio, "A Realist Ethos of Resistance in Global
Politics," Global Studies Quarterly, Volume 4, Issue 2, April 2024, dml)

This article argues that [Realism contains an implicit ethos of
**resistance**]{.underline}, [which **reverses** the conventional image
of the tradition as **coldly accepting** the powerful dominating the
weak]{.underline}. Uncovering this ethos, however, requires three shifts
of perspective immanent to Realist debates. Disputes over the
possibility and significance of these shifts are longstanding and yet
have been the focus of much recent scholarship aimed at critically
reinterpreting a "classical" Realism in contrast to structuralist and
conservative variants. These shifts are (1) from an extreme to moderate
view of power politics; (2) from naturalizing the status quo to
envisaging progressive change from within the political domain; and (3)
from a horizontal view of politics among nations (or other like
entities) to a global view of power politics. In this article, a Realist
ethos of resistance is also articulated through a parallel engagement
with the philosopher Bernard Williams. His anti-moralist theory of
legitimate political power is an influential resource in new Realist
political theory. I extend his key concepts to a wider discussion---in
both IR and political theory---of the tradition's implicit ethos of
resistance. By engaging with classical Realist themes in IR in tandem
with Williams' ideas, [this]{.underline} article [illustrates Realism's
significance as a **challenger** to apologies for **domination** and
**injustice**]{.underline}. The contribution of this article is to
position a Realist voice about the realities of power politics in recent
debates over human rights to resist in global politics. Together,
classical and "new" [Realisms are **relevant** because they can
theorize]{.underline} such rights [within the context of knowledge about
the conditions and limits of legitimate power in **real-world
struggles**]{.underline}, [rather than **moralizing** resistance in the
**abstract**]{.underline}.

[This]{.underline} argument for a Realist ethos of resistance [builds
upon **extensive scholarship**]{.underline}, in both IR and political
theory, [**undercutting** the notion that Realism is a doctrine
of]{.underline} "**[might makes right]{.underline}**." As Vibeke Schou
Tjalve and Michael C. Williams state, "[From its
**origins**]{.underline}, political [realism has been based on being
**sociologically realistic** and **ethically responsible**]{.underline}"
(Tjalve and Williams 2015, 58). [The argument also builds on the
recovery of intellectual resources]{.underline}, [from **within** the
tradition]{.underline}, [concerning **morally illegitimate** forms and
uses of power]{.underline}, [in addition to the political ethics of
resistance struggles **against domination** and
**injustices**]{.underline}. That scholarship uncovers how
[exemplar]{.underline} mid-twentieth century classical
[Realists]{.underline}, such as Hans J. Morgenthau and Reinhold Niebuhr,
[**insisted** on the need to]{.underline}, in Morgenthau's words,
"[**regulate** and **restrain** the power drives that otherwise would
either tear society apart or deliver the weak to the arbitrary will of
the powerful]{.underline}" (Morgenthau 1985, 244). Indeed, [classical
Realist meditations on restraint and the limitation of power extend to
the **inevitability** and **necessity** of bottom-up challenges to
domination]{.underline} (Tjalve 2008; Hartnett 2021). This literature
complements recent applications of Realist ideas to the philosophy and
tactics of civil resistance and civil disobedience (Mantena 2012; King
2018; Sabl 2021). Building on these interventions, I develop a concept
of an ethos of resistance to frame and ground legitimate resistance
within the frictions and disagreements of the broader tradition, and
then situate a Realist voice in contemporary debates about resistance
rights in an era of global injustices.

This article proceeds in five steps: First, I outline how [the
tradition's core tenets are **not fixed**]{.underline}, and yet can
still be represented as a conversation about knowledge and truth about
the "reality" of politics and the challenges of formulating a political
morality appropriate to this reality. Second, I define Realism's
implicit relationship to an ethos of resistance through examples, both
the canonical and the unexpected, to provide a glimpse---and initial
description---of Realism's ethos. Third, I show how [an ethos of
resistance aligns with]{.underline} three **[shifts]{.underline}**
immanent to debates [within]{.underline} classical and "new"
[Realisms]{.underline} noted above---[toward
**moderation**]{.underline}, [embracing **progressive political
change**]{.underline}, [and a **global view** of power
relations]{.underline}. Fourth, I reinterpret the Realist philosopher
Bernard Williams' key concepts of the first political question and the
Basic Legitimation Demand as illustrations of an ethos of resistance
(Williams 2005). Finally, I situate this ethos in recent literatures
concerning a universal human right to resist domination and injustice.
By identifying Realism's internal resources for an ethos of resistance,
I show how the tradition both complements and critiques recent
non-Realist scholarship. Rather than advancing an applied ethics of
resistance, a Realist ethos focuses on how politics is about legitimate
power in contextual and real-world struggles.

A preliminary comment on resistance is in order. In this article,
resistance refers to a variety of political acts resulting from a
capacity to withstand or limit power (Barbalet 1985, 531). Resistance
comes in many forms and varies according to the nature of the power it
seeks to limit (Barnett and Duvall 2005). Depending on the context,
resisters seek a diversity of actions and outcomes, including
withdrawing cooperation and labor in support of a particular political
and social order, mobilizing political opposition, undermining a foreign
occupation, terrorism, and causing a revolution resulting in regime
change.1 In all its forms, resistance contests the legitimacy of power.
Acknowledging the variety of resistances, this article starts and ends
with a broad conception, and then narrows the topic by focusing on the
illegitimacy of domination.

What is Realism? From Axioms to Two Axes

[Realism is]{.underline} an "artificial construct" built from
asynchronous conversations about politics (Forde 1992, 62). As Jonathan
Haslam states, it is [a]{.underline} "[**spectrum** of
ideas]{.underline}" [and **not**]{.underline} "[a **fixed point of
focus** with sharp definition]{.underline}" (Haslam 2002, 249). In IR,
the history of political thought, and political theory, many seek to
define Realism's core tenets or axioms. Three general axioms from the
literature are (1) that politics should be understood on its own terms,
rather than through moral, idealistic, or religious categories (Galston
2010, 408; Finlayson 2017, 267); (2) that politics results from and
reflects intractable realities, namely "disagreement, contestation, and
coercion" (Rossi and Sleat 2014, 691; see also McQueen 2017, 297). And
therefore, (3) descriptively and prescriptively, Realists analyze the
"primacy of self-interest over moral principle" as a "necessity, and
therefore as a right" (Forde 1992, 62). It is possible to identify other
and more specific axioms but doing so risks having to concede exceptions
and internal disagreements. Rather than ignore the impact of these
divergences and incongruities, some point to the advantages of a
"reflexive" approach to Realist theorizing. Here, as Brent J. Steele
notes, "Reflexive [realism does not]{.underline}. . .[seek to
**reify**]{.underline} central modern realist [assumptions of
**power**]{.underline}, **[anarchy]{.underline}**, [or even]{.underline}
'**[the state]{.underline}**' [as **absolute**]{.underline} principles
of international politics, [but instead as contexualized artefacts that
**can** and **have changed**]{.underline}" (Steele 2007, 276--7).
Similarly, as Geróid Brinn argues, the problem in defining Realism in
relation to "shared tenets" is that "[even when different realists do
have shared commitments]{.underline}, [they will often mean **very
different things** to each]{.underline}" (Brinn 2020, 211).

To advance my argument about a Realist ethos of resistance, I focus on
two axes as virtual lines of conversation---both in agreement and
dispute---over the axioms that are commonly asserted:

Axis 1 (A1): Realism gravitates around the goal of advancing knowledge
claims about the fundamental nature of the political and power;

Axis 2 (A2): Realism confronts and advances questions of political
morality not in the abstract, but within the logic of A1 knowledge
claims.

The A1 axis involves claims about the nature and effects of the "given"
or limiting features of politics, and includes inter alia: human nature
and psychology; the insecurities and fears actors have in conditions of
anarchy or insecurity; and conflict and disagreement over fundamental
interests, values, and identities. An example of an A1 statement by a
classical Realist is Hans J. Morgenthau's: "the truth of political
science is the truth about power, its manifestations, its
configurations, its limitations, implications, its laws" (quoted in
Cozette 2008, 10). Another example from new Realist political theory is
Bernard Williams' assertion of the "universalism of negative
capabilities." He states, "the only certainly universal material of
politics: \[is\] power, powerlessness, fear and cruelty" (2005, 59). As
exemplars of classical and new Realisms, [neither Morgenthau nor
Williams assert that we must simply **take the world as it
is**]{.underline}. Rather, [knowledge claims are entangled with
practical and moral questions framed by the political]{.underline}; A1
statements raise A2 questions. [Seeking to **avoid wishful
thinking**]{.underline}, **[infeasible hopes]{.underline}**, [and
**abstract doctrines**]{.underline}, [Realists]{.underline}' A2
[discourse is]{.underline} distinctive.

Morgenthau follows his statement above [about uncovering
the]{.underline} "**[truth]{.underline}**" [about power's
manifestations]{.underline}, [configurations]{.underline},
[limitations]{.underline}, [and laws]{.underline}, with the A2 statement
that a Realist must tell "power what it can do and what it ought to do,
what is feasible and what is required" (quoted in Cozette 2008, 10).
Similarly, as William Galston writes, new "[Realists begin from where a
given political community **is**]{.underline}, [they assess the
**strength** of the bulwarks against the great evils of the human
condition that community has erected]{.underline}, [and they formulate
the **next steps** based on that assessment]{.underline}" (2010, 396).
At the same time, there are internal frictions within Realism because
the "universal" materials of politics (A1) and "next steps" are both
contextual and contested (A2). Defined in terms of a set of typical
axioms, Realism lacks a clearcut, paradigmatic, or shared perspective on
legitimate resistance to domination. However, when the tradition is also
understood relative to longstanding conversations about (A1) knowledge
claims of political reality, and (A2) the practical and moral
engagements that arise, we can see glimpses of an ethos of resistance.

Glimpses of a Realist Ethos of Resistance

Glimpses of a Realist ethos of resistance appear in revisiting and
reappraising some dominant narratives of the tradition. Thucydides'
famous "Realist" account of a dialogue in the Peloponnesian War
(431--404 B.C.E.) is often misunderstood (see Lebow 2003, 35). The
backdrop is Athens' wider war with Sparta. In the conversation between
the powerful Athenian generals and the weaker Melians, the former
demanded allegiance and submission. The Melian leaders maintain a right
of neutrality and independence, as a matter of justice (Thucydides 2008,
567). The Athenians' notorious response is that, among those unequal in
power, "the strong do what they can. . .the weak suffer what they must"
(2008, 566--7). When the dialogue ends, it is understood that the
Athenians "besieged the place" with the Melians being killed and
enslaved for their resistance (2008, 576).

The meaning and results of these words and actions are often depicted as
an instance of Realist intellectual and moral "honesty" (Forde 1992,
72). But "Thucydides leads his readers to regard this \[slaughter of the
Melians\] as an act of barbarism---that is, as morally reprehensible in
some sense," and one that eventually leads to Athens' breakdown into
"factious strife" (Forde 1992, 73). As Richard Ned Lebow argues,
Thucydides' narrative is not reducible to propositional axioms about
power, and the supposed futility of resistance. Rather, "Greek tragedy
and classical realists had a different understanding of knowledge" or
"tacit political knowledge," which engages "our intellect and emotions"
(Lebow 2003, 59). As he writes,

Thucydides is using the Dialogue to tell us that raw force can impose
its will at any moment, but no empire has the military and economic
capability to repress its subjects indefinitely. Allies who regard
themselves the objects of exploitation will rebel when the opportunity
arises. Oppression also leaves memories that inhibit future attempts at
empire building. (Lebow 2003, 125)

Indeed, an over-extended Athenian power, applied with needless cruelty,
was undercut by a deeper crisis of legitimacy (Lebow 2003, 216).

Many including Lebow highlight the parallels between Thucydides and
Morgenthau's Realist opposition to the Vietnam War in the 1960s (Lebow
2003, 125; Cozette 2008, 16--8; Scheuerman 2009, 164--95; Zambernardi
2011). Morgenthau believed it was unrealistic for the United States to
expect to convert its superior military power into victory in Vietnam,
and the elimination of communist influence in the region. But crucially,
he took seriously that the Vietnamese resistance was part of a longer
"national" struggle to drive out foreign and colonial dominators
(Morgenthau 1968, 32; Lebow 2003, 240; Zambernardi 2011, 1340). For the
Vietnamese, fighting for national independence was a grave interest,
although they were divided on how to achieve it. By contrast, the United
States had no similarly grave interest in the fight, despite what its
leaders claimed (Lebow 2003, 240). From an American perspective,
Morgenthau believed the war was unnecessary and could only undermine
influence among allies and national unity (Scheuerman 2009, 171--2).

These two widely discussed Realist vignettes are important examples of
disputes among Realists over core axioms. Consider the axiom of the
primacy of self-interest over moral principle. Were the Athenian
generals being "Realists" when they asserted that their self-interest
and superior power required dispensing with any notion of "justice"? Or
was Thucydides' observation that Athens' extreme and infamous slaughter
of the Melians epitomized a loss of ethical balance and restraint that
many believe should define the tradition? Morgenthau's Thucydides-like
"Realist" critique of the American War in Vietnam clashed with others
who saw the conflict as a necessary response to Cold War geopolitical
struggles, and with vital national interests at stake (Zambernardi 2011,
1352--3). Such disagreement raises again the awkward question of whose
Realism is more attuned to the "real" A1 truths and knowledge claims.
Morgenthau's protest of the American War on Vietnamese resisters has
become a new cautionary tale, particularly in the scholarship on [the
tradition's]{.underline} classical, [reflexive history]{.underline}, and
contemporary relevance (Lebow 2003, 234--54; Williams 2005, 193; Steele
2007, 286; Tjalve 2008, 2, 75, 118).

These two well-known vignettes also [offer suggestive glimpses
of]{.underline} this article's central concept, an ethos of
resistance---[an implicit attitude that resistance is a **legitimate
response** to domination by the powerful]{.underline}. Karuna Mantena's
article on Mohandas [**Gandhi**'s activism against British colonial rule
provides another]{.underline}, unexpected [glimpse of such an
ethos]{.underline}. He depicts Gandhi's leadership and philosophy as
[premised on]{.underline} "another" kind of **[Realism]{.underline}**
(2012, 457). Inverting the stereotype of naïve moralism, Mantena asserts
that key texts and events show a much different picture: "[Gandhi\'s
realist theory of politics was a **contextual**]{.underline},
**[consequentialist]{.underline}**, and moral-psychological [analysis of
a political world understood to be marked by inherent tendencies toward
conflict]{.underline}, domination, and violence" (Mantena 2012, 459).
[Gandhi focused on how non-violence is a]{.underline} "[**practical
orientation** and a set of **strategic responses** rather than simply an
**ethical stance** or standard of moral judgement]{.underline}" (Mantena
2012). Quite clearly, Gandhi was more than a dispassionate historical
observer, like Thucydides, and exercised an influence different than an
activist--scholar--citizen like Morgenthau, who was frustrated by his
country's reckless imposition of power in Southeast Asia. Yet Mantena
shows that Gandhi's leadership was based on a dispassionate
interpretation of the human passions and inclinations, such as pride,
honor, and reactivity to cycles of violence (along the A1 axis). At the
same time, Mantena's research shows how Gandhi dissolved the distinction
between the A1 and A2 axes: The active demonstration of truth
(satyagraha) through disciplined and restrained non-cooperation
demonstrated the irrational contradictions and illegitimacy of British
colonial rule (Mantena 2012, 462--4). This study recasts the events
surrounding Gandhi's moral leadership beyond their well-known effects on
transforming the real-world politics of colonial domination, as Realist
rather than moralistic. I suggest they also offer a glimpse of what is
meant here by an ethos of resistance.

I use [the term **ethos**]{.underline} to [underline that Realism's
political morality **eschews** a **universalist**]{.underline},
[**extra-political** concept of right and wrong that **takes
precedence** over the real-world political realm]{.underline}. Instead,
[an ethos is a disposition and attitude to this realm]{.underline}.
Indeed, recent scholarship on classical IR Realism often employs the
term ethos to point to distinctive versions of a non-moralistic
political morality.2 As Mark T. Lilla writes, [an ethos can inform and
overlay a morality or ethical code]{.underline}, [but]{.underline} by
doing so, it [becomes]{.underline} "[**more** than **abstract
reasoning**]{.underline}" (Lilla 1981, 14). Indeed, "\[e\]thical theory
is really the furthest thing from an ethos. . .\[[which
tends]{.underline}\] [towards abstract logical systems **quite
divorced** from the lives we live]{.underline}" (Lilla 1981, 10). [The
term can also be related to]{.underline} Max Weber's political ethos,
characterized by skepticism of moral absolutism and [recognition of the
need to]{.underline} "[make **consequentialist calculations** to help
determine the appropriateness of particular political
means]{.underline}" (Satkunanandan 2014, 169). Such an ethos accepts the
"'ethical irrationality' of the world and thereby rise\[s\] to the
fundamental, substantively unspecifiable, and inexhaustible
responsibility \[of political actors\] to respond to the world 'as it
really is'" (Satkunanandan 2014).

This article suggests that an ethos of resistance in Realism overlays
the specific political and ethical judgments and decisions of scholars
and actors. With such an ethos, resistance is understood as a blameless
response to those responsible for domination and injustices, rather than
as futile action. But not all Realists would adopt such an ethos; and in
the next section, I show how it only comes into focus within the
tradition because of three choices.

Three Realist Choices: Moderate, Progressive, and Global

These initial glimpses of a Realist ethos of resistance correspond with
three choices within the tradition's history. The ethos is activated by:
(1) choosing political moderation over extremism; (2) an openness to
progressive change to a status quo versus conserving extant power
arrangements; and (3) an expansiveness with respect to theorizing the
significance of resistance in politics, locating it globally rather than
horizontally among states or vertically between a state and its
subjects.

Extremist and Moderate Realisms

The most significant internal divide between Realists is often described
as the extremist versus moderate orientations to politics (A1) and the
morality (A2) of action (see Forde 1992; Mantena 2012, 455; McQueen
2017, 298). At the extremist end of the spectrum, Realism endorses might
makes right, a maxim that moderates reject in favor of making judgments
about the need to balance and modulate conflicting political objectives.

Extremist Realism argues that natural or objective forces create an
unyielding reality of struggles for power. In such struggles, those with
more power possess a "right" to dominate the weak (as an A1 claim). The
Athenian generals tell the Melians, "You know as well as we do that
right, as the world goes, is only in question between equals in power"
(Thucydides 2008, 566). As Walzer (1977, 5) observes, the logic of this
argument is that political necessity narrows and bifurcates the
political: For Athens, it is "reign or be subject," and for the Melians,
it is "yield or be destroyed." Extremist Realism "claim\[s\] to have
discovered an awful truth: what we conventionally call inhumanity is
simply humanity under pressure" (Walzer 1977, 4). Indeed, contemporary
structural Realists such as John Mearsheimer appeal to the so-called
awful truth to explain if not justify great power domination and
aggression: "The sad fact is that international politics has always been
a ruthless and dangerous business, and it is likely to remain that
way....the international system forces states which seek only to be
secure to nonetheless act aggressively toward each other" (Mearsheimer
2014, 2, 3). Recently, he applied this view to Russia's annexation and
invasions of Ukraine, as understandable responses to Western powers'
behavior, namely, attempts to check or limit Russia's sphere of
dominance (i.e., expanding NATO's membership) (Mearsheimer 2022). Not
unlike Athens' atrocities in Melos, Russia's attacks on Ukrainian
civilian targets are extreme. But for extreme Realists, real-world
politics will necessarily involve atrocities inflicted on the weak.

Challenging extremist Realist arguments is a necessary step to
identifying how and where an ethos of resistance can fit in the broader
tradition. Ido Oren (2009) challenges the contradictions in structural
Realism's approach to the relationship of supposedly objective realities
and prescriptive advocacy. On the one hand, Mearsheimer (2014, 3) argues
that the objective realities of power and anarchy cannot be altered to
appease an extraneous ideology or morality, such as justice or human
rights (Mearsheimer 2014, 46--8). On the other hand, when real-world
actors---whether victims or third-party powers---resist or push back,
the extremist version of structural Realism asserts prescriptive
language against irrational behavior, i.e., at odds with the actors'
objective interests. But as Oren asks, "if there\'s a 'real world'
impervious to political thought, why bother to try to influence it?"
(2009, 283). After all, the Athenians generals try to convince the
Melians that it would be better for all concerned if they simply
capitulated. As well, Mearsheimer criticizes Western policymakers for
insensitivity to Russia's geopolitical anxieties about NATO's eastward
expansion (Mearsheimer 2022). Unless significantly amended, extremist
Realism's descriptive (A1) and prescriptive faces (A2) become
paradoxical, leading to an "impossibility of being a consistent and
thoroughgoing realist" (Oren 2009, 296).

This impossibility is augmented by extremist Realism's ideas concerning
political (a)morality. As Mantena suggests, "if politics is understood
as determining, partly or wholly, its own internal standards, it opens
the door to harder edged realisms that dispense with the category of
morality altogether" (2012, 346). By fetishizing the strong's
ability/"right" to dominate the weak, extreme Realists go far beyond
steering clear of naive moralism. Instead, they advocate a kind of
solipsist amoralism for the most powerful actors. The position of the
Athenian generals is that the Melians ought to "have the good sense to
submit to the inevitable domination of the stronger" rather than
"moralizing the supposed 'injustice' of it" (Forde 1992, 72). Submitting
to the stronger power "without resistance. . .\[would minimize\] the
suffering on both sides" (Forde 1992). With such claims, and to Walzer's
point, extreme Realism hollows out and eviscerates any possibilities for
the meaningful moral argumentation (along A2).

[Closer examination of the tradition reveals the persistence of a
moderate alternative rooted in classical Realism]{.underline}. For
moderates, [politics is **not reducible** to the power to **dominate**
and **coerce** others]{.underline} (Lebow 2003, 275, 310; Barkin 2010,
19--20). Instead, [politics must entail perceptions of authority or
legitimacy]{.underline}, [as well as leadership or political
judgment]{.underline}, [which together provide
opportunities]{.underline}---[and responsibilities]{.underline}---[to
**temper** the most flagrant of excessive impulses of
power]{.underline}. Indeed, for moderates, [unrestrained
power]{.underline}, [and flagrant injustices **undermine** the
fundamental]{.underline} political and social [purposes that power is
meant to create and secure]{.underline}.3 While structuralist Realism
remains a dominant IR approach, a recent generation of reflexive,
broadly constructivist research shows how the tradition's history is
heavily populated with the arguments of moderate Realists. Thucydides,
Hobbes, Clausewitz, Morgenthau, and other classical Realists theorized
how the tensions among politics, power, legitimacy, and resistance
cannot be short-circuited in favor of extremist actions (Lebow 2003, 35;
Steele 2007, 273; Hom and Steele 2010, 272; Levine 2012, 26; Tjalve and
Williams 2015, 39; Hom 2018, 139). There is also a literature uncovering
how [many classical Realist reactions to power are rooted
in]{.underline} religious [conceptions of **human finitude** and
**social justice**]{.underline}, [as well as]{.underline} early modern
European and republican conceptions of [the need to **check** and
**limit** uncontrolled power and domination]{.underline} (Rosenthal
1991; Murray 1996; Williams 2005, 6, 18; Tjalve 2008, 5, 16; Tjalve and
Williams 2015, 39--40; Hartnett 2021). Indeed, [a moderate Realism
theorizes power in terms of legitimacy and limitation]{.underline}, [and
this introduces an **accepting**]{.underline}, [if not
**radical**]{.underline}, [advocacy for resistance to
domination]{.underline}. Moreover, [moderates see resistance to abuses
and injustices as **important** and **consequential**]{.underline},
[rather than futile and irrelevant]{.underline}.

Status Quo and Progressive Realisms

[It is axiomatic for Realism that]{.underline} disagreement, conflict,
and [power struggles cannot be eliminated through instituting a
universal set of moral values]{.underline} and legal norms. [But it is
an **ongoing debate** whether this skepticism]{.underline} ultimately
[reinforces status quo power relations]{.underline} and institutions,
[or whether Realism can envisage **progressive** and even **radical**
change]{.underline} (Finlayson 2017; Goodhart 2018, 81--106; Brinn
2020). As Duncan Bell writes, [Realism is]{.underline}
"**[not]{.underline}** (in any of its usual variants) [a **fully
fledged** political ideology]{.underline}, [with coherent and
determinate positions]{.underline} on a wide range of moral and
political issues" (Bell 2009, 12). Instead, [the tradition is a
**conversation** among ideologically and politically diverse
standpoints]{.underline}. Realist disagreement over the necessity and
possibility of reconciling power politics with purposeful social and
political change, even progress, occurs along both A1 and A2.

[In **contrast** to stereotypes of Realists as **complacent** and
**conservative**]{.underline}, [recent scholarship has shown how **key
thinkers** of the tradition sought **feasible challenges** to existing
power relations and institutions]{.underline}. For instance, Michael
Williams' (2005) category of "wilful" Realists, illustrated by Thomas
Hobbes, J.-J. Rousseau, and Morgenthau, paints a different picture. For
these thinkers, being realistic requires a political social construction
project; the constitution of actors capable of generating conditions for
legitimacy in liberal modernity (2005, 16, 20). Similarly,
**[Scheuerman]{.underline}** (2011) [contrasts more
**conservative-minded** Realists]{.underline}, who prioritize skepticism
of change to the point where support for a status quo is the only
"realistic" choice, [with **progressive** Realists]{.underline},
[who]{.underline} advocate for significant, yet gradual reforms.
Twentieth-century progressive Realists include E.H. Carr, Reinhold
Niebuhr, and Hans J. Morgenthau. Unlike the latter-day conservatives
Scheuerman mentions (such as Henry Kissinger, Kenneth Waltz, and
Mearsheimer), this earlier wave of progressives [argued that Realism
**errs** when it tracks **too closely** with the interests of status quo
power arrangements]{.underline}. For one thing, [endorsing a status quo
of great power relations **increases the possibility** of **nuclear**
and other **atrocities**]{.underline}. [The very **survival** of the
species]{.underline} and of the political domain, one for which there
are opportunities to preserve and advance human interests, [is at
stake]{.underline}. But [there is a more comprehensive set of
progressive interests at stake in **challenging injustices** and
**inequalities**]{.underline}.

On political and moral grounds, [classical Realists have **critiqued**
efforts to conserve **perverse power inequalities** or **social
domination**]{.underline}. Dissent and resistance to unjust power
structures were key themes in both Niebuhr and Morgenthau's
interventions. To be clear, neither claimed nor advocated that
revolutionary politics or violence is the best remedy for oppression or
domination. Yet [they **firmly rejected** passively accepting an unjust
status quo]{.underline}. [**Niebuhr** wrote against moralistic and
religious pacifisms that acquiesce to violent oppression]{.underline},
[whether in the]{.underline} **[U]{.underline}**nited
**[S]{.underline}**tates, [with respect to **racial
discrimination**]{.underline}, [or **globally** in respect to
**imperialism**]{.underline} (Scheuerman 2011, 22; Hartnett 2021, 161).
As he wrote in his 1932 Moral Man and Immoral Society: "When collective
power, whether in the form of imperialism or class domination, exploits
weakness, it can never be dislodged unless power is raised against it.
If conscience and reason can be insinuated into the resulting struggle
they can only qualify but not abolish it" (Niebuhr 2021, 6). Similarly,
Morgenthau argued that "social conflict remained permanent features of
the human condition" and that justice and equilibrium among conflicting
groups were required to "eliminate the incentive to seek justice through
strife" (Scheuerman 2011, 24). [Far from a **complacent acceptance** of
power realities]{.underline}, [**Morgenthau** argued that a Realist
theory]{.underline} "\[a\]t its very best. . .[**cannot help** being a
**subversive** and **revolutionary** force]{.underline} with regard to
certain vested interests---intellectual, political, economic, social in
general."4Mantena's (2012) analysis of Gandhi's activist philosophy
echoes Scheuerman and others' treatments of mid-century American and
European progressive Realists. [For such Realism]{.underline},
[progressive change is **possible** and **necessary** for countering
injustices]{.underline}, [whether caused by **great power
hubris**]{.underline}, **[capitalism]{.underline}**,
**[colonialism]{.underline}**, [or other forces of **social
domination**]{.underline}.5

### AT: Root Cause

#### Great power war doesn't have a root cause, or it would occur [all the time]{.underline}, not [twice per century]{.underline}.

**Clare, 21**---Research Fellow, Forethought Foundation for Global
Priorities Research (Stephen, "Great Power Conflict," *Founders Pledge*,
November 2021, dml)

Having clarified what is meant by terms \"Great Power\", we now turn our
attention to the literature on the causes of war. [[Great Power war
is]{.underline}]{.mark} an intrinsically
**[[difficult]{.underline}]{.mark}** subject [[to

are]{.mark}]{.underline} (fortunately) relatively
**[[rare]{.underline}]{.mark}**: [humanity has averaged **two** major
wars per century for the last **500 years**]{.underline}. [They also
have **many possible causes**]{.underline}. Nevertheless, researchers
have made many [attempts to]{.underline} tackle this issue and [identify
factors that **increase** or **decrease** the risk]{.underline} of wars
breaking out. These [are exercises in **probability**]{.underline}:
while factors that guarantee war have not been found, these research
efforts have identified many characteristics, contexts, and correlates
that make conflicts more or less likely.

Levels of analysis

[Many possible causes of war have been proposed]{.underline}. To
organize our analysis, we adopt a framework used by both Greg Cashman in
What Causes War and Jack Levy and William Thompson in Causes of War. The
[possible causes]{.underline} of war [are]{.underline} sorted into five
categories: the 28 **[individual]{.underline}** level, the
**[sub-state]{.underline}** level, the **[state]{.underline}** level,
the **[bilateral]{.underline}** level, [and]{.underline} the [**system**
level]{.underline}. [[**Different** causes]{.mark} of war [apply]{.mark}
in **different situations**]{.underline}, [**[multiple]{.mark}** causes
might exert an [influence]{.mark} in [the **same
situation**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[and]{.mark} there may be

empirical evidence behind each of these causes are considered.

[**Rarely** does all the evidence point in the same
direction]{.underline}; instead, [a **range** of different causes apply
to different historical or hypothetical cases]{.underline}. [Studies can
also produce **different**]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} [sometimes
**contradictory** results]{.underline}, [depending on how they define
and operationalize concepts]{.underline}. Nevertheless, the **[evidence
[reviews]{.mark}]{.underline}** from Cashman and Levy and Thompson
[[highlight]{.underline}]{.mark} some important findings. In general,
territorial disputes, **[Great Power [tensions]{.mark}]{.underline}**,
ideological conflicts, psychological biases, [[and
**escalatory**]{.mark} **foreign [policy]{.mark}
responses**]{.underline} all seem to [[**raise** danger]{.mark}
levels]{.underline}. In contrast, [the spread of
**[democracy]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [**de-escalation** of
rivalries]{.underline}, [and **resolution** of]{.underline} minor
[disputes]{.underline} like competing territorial claims seem to
[[advance **peace**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

An important caveat for all that follows is that it is difficult to know
when findings based on studies of conflicts in the past can be
generalized to make predictions about conflicts in the future.

Individual level

Researchers working at the individual level try to explain war [by
studying]{.underline} the nature and
**[[psychology]{.underline}]{.mark}** of people, especially the heads of
state who decide whether or not to take their country into war. Many
[thinkers]{.underline}, from St. Augustine of Hippo to Sigmund Freud,
[have hypothesized that war results from an **innate aggressiveness**
within humans]{.underline}. **[However]{.underline}**, [the **empirical
evidence** for this claim is **weak**]{.underline}. [Though
anthropologists]{.underline}, [historians]{.underline}, [and political
scientists debate the **roots** of]{.underline} violence and
[warfare]{.underline}, [they **generally agree** that aggression is
**mediated** by]{.underline} the [social and environmental
contexts]{.underline} in which people live.29 Moreover, [attributing the
existence of war to innate aggression [does **little** to explain why
wars occur **some**]{.mark}]{.underline}, [[but **not
all**]{.underline}]{.mark}, [[of the time]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Researchers have identified **[several [features]{.mark}]{.underline}**
of state leaders [[that explain]{.mark} some of the
**[variance]{.mark}** in the incidence of warfare]{.underline}. The
cognitive revolution of the last several decades, which has emphasized
the role of subconscious and emotional processes in individual
decision-making, has undermined the idea that rational reasoning alone
determines outcomes in international relations.30 Factors that play a
role [[include]{.underline}]{.mark}:

**[[Personality]{.mark} traits]{.underline}**: Several studies have
presented evidence that leaders with more authoritarian, domineering,
extroverted, or risk-tolerant personalities are more likely to advocate
for the use of force, 31 though this literature is too thin to generate
any reliable conclusions about the size of this effect.

[**Heuristics** and **[biases]{.mark}**]{.underline}: Leaders use
shortcuts and simple models to make decisions, such as relying on
historical analogies to assess situations.32 Several experts believe
leaders are systematically overconfident when making decisions about
war, especially once the war has started or seems inevitable.

**[[Misperception]{.underline}]{.mark}**: It is difficult to accurately
obtain and process decision-relevant information during times of
conflict.34 Given this, leaders may be overconfident in their analyses
and predisposed to misperceive their opponents as aggressive yet
vulnerable.

**[[Stress]{.underline}]{.mark}**: Finally, biases are heightened during
times of stress, when people find it even more difficult to reason
systematically and clearly. Most, but not all, studies suggest that
stress has a significant, negative impact on decision-making ability
during times of conflict.35

Since many of these factors, such as personality traits or
stress-tolerance, vary among individuals, they can explain some of the
variance in the incidence of war. [A jingoistic leader **alone** can
make a state more likely to go to war]{.underline}.
**[However]{.underline}**, [**many other factors** at levels **beyond**
the individual also bear on this outcome]{.underline}, starting with the
influence of group decision-making processes.
